I’m comfortable that the alpha beta algorithm runs reasonably fast and select the best raw earned differential at the end of the round. This is good and I’m happy with that part. What we now need is something that adjusts that raw earned differential into a better value. The classic example as seen in all high level play is trashing the last single tile on the final move rather than starting a new pattern lines that doesn’t connect with the rest of your wall. -1 for that single floor tile is often well worth it to keep a pattern lines empty (clean) for the start of the next round where you can be more opportunistic. It’s almost like I don’t care very much about policy priors I care instead about the value of the position. Then we alpha beta (minimax) our way to the best state.  AB has it easy because its value it known, with an adjusted value the value is less certain, and would change generation to generation as the bot learns.

11:31 AM
Claude responded: Got it.

Got it. This is crystal clear now. You need a value function learner, not a policy learner. The policy (which move to pick) is already solved by AlphaBeta—it's fast and correct. What's broken is the evaluation of positions.

Let me first answer your question, then lay out a concrete plan.
What is RL?

RL = Reinforcement Learning. The agent learns by trial and error: take actions, get rewards, update its model to maximize future reward. Three flavors relevant here:

    Value iteration — learn V(state) = expected future score from this state onward. No policy; just values. Update rule: V(s) ← reward(s) + discount × max V(s') where s' are reachable next states.
    Policy gradient — learn a policy π(a|s) directly. Sample moves, measure how good they were, push probability mass toward good moves.
    Actor-Critic — learn both policy and value; policy suggests moves, value judges them.

AlphaZero is actor-critic: policy head picks moves (via MCTS visit counts), value head judges positions. The problem: your policy head hasn't learned meaningful patterns yet, so MCTS visit distributions are near-random, so training targets are corrupted before they reach the value head.

What you need: Pure value learning. No policy head at all.
The Plan: Value-Only Learning
Core idea

    Use AlphaBeta to generate training data. Run AB at depth 3–4 on thousands of random mid-game positions. AB's answer = ground truth: V_AB(position).
    Train a value net to match AB's evaluations. Supervised learning: net(encoding) ≈ AB_value. No RL, no MCTS, no self-play. Just regression.
    Integrate the value net back into AlphaBeta. Replace the hand-coded eval with value_net(position). This is AlphaBeta with learned eval.
    Iterate. Once gen-1 net is trained, use it in AlphaBeta to generate gen-2 data, train gen-2 net, etc. Each generation should beat the previous one.
    End state: An AlphaBeta search that uses a neural value head instead of hand-coded evaluation. Should reach 90%+ vs AB hard.

Why this works

    AlphaBeta is deterministic and fast. It produces consistent, high-quality supervision labels (AB's own evaluation).
    Value learning is a supervised problem, not an RL problem. No credit assignment horror. Just: does your net match AB's judgments?
    Generational improvement is automatic. Gen-2 net sees AB-with-gen-1-net evaluations; if gen-1 net is better than hand-coded eval, gen-2 sees better supervision.
    No policy learning. You avoid the entire 3-head factorization mess, policy target corruption, floor-move overweighting, etc.
    Natural curriculum. Early generations learn to match AB's coarse judgments (winning vs losing). Later generations learn fine distinctions (board structure, line cleanliness, color clustering).

Concrete Implementation (2–3 weeks wall time)
Phase A: Data generation (Day 1–2)

Expected trajectory
Generation	vs hand-coded AB	vs AB hard	Notes
Hand-coded (baseline)	50%	~45%	Baseline
Gen 0	~55–60%	~48–52%	Net learns to match AB at depth 3
Gen 1	~60–65%	~55–60%	Better supervision → better net
Gen 2–3	~70–80%	~70–80%	Compounding improvement
Gen 4+	~85–95%	~85–95%	Potential superhuman territory

Each generation takes ~1 hour (10min data + 30min train + 20min eval). So 10 generations ≈ 10 hours wall time.
Why this beats AlphaZero for your goal

    Supervision is reliable. AlphaBeta's evaluation is consistent, not noisy self-play data.
    No policy confusion. You're not fighting a policy head that doesn't know what it's doing.
    Interpretable progress. Each generation's win rate clearly shows whether the net improved.
    Fast iteration. Generational loop is days, not weeks.
    Aligns with your insight. You identified that the real problem is evaluation (position value), not move selection (policy). This directly addresses that.

Open questions for you

    How bad is AlphaBeta's hand-coded eval currently? Can you quantify the "leaves too much on the table" part? E.g., is it missing obvious clean-line tactics, or is it more subtle?
    Does your current encoder (125-value flat vector) capture the right features? (walls, pattern lines, tile availability) Or do you suspect it's missing something important (e.g., "cleanness" of a pattern line)?
    Can we run AlphaBeta at depth 4 in <1sec? If so, we can use that as ground truth. If it takes 5+ seconds, depth 3 is more practical for 10k training examples.

Ready to pick this up?
You said: Hand-coded eval is strictly earned score differential.

Hand-coded eval is strictly earned score differential. It was the most basic eval that makes sense at every single move. It always has signal. It doesn’t need to wait till round or game end to know how well it did. Deeper just looks further ahead.
I could guesstimate a few tweaks to this such as extra value for holding the first player tile. Extra value for keeping line clean. Extra value for playing in the center where future round have more adjacency opportunities. …
The current encoder capture the game state, with very little extra. We can definitely engineer some additional features (doubling or even more) the count. They would add a bunch of this should be useful information to the encoding but again the model could choose to ignore it if it’s not useful. 
3 we’ll have to bench mark it to see. On the first turn of the game there are often up to 100 legal moves. So branching deep early takes time. When we get towards the end of a round the move count drastically shifts to much more manageable numbers. Because of this we have AB take both a depth and threshold value. It first determines the maximum number of turns left in the round. If that is over the threshold then it search at the prescribed depth. If there are less turns remaining in the round then it fully search’s to terminal nodes(which is the number of turns left in the round)

Phase A.1: Feature Engineering (Today)

You mentioned several good features. Let me sketch what to add and where:

Current encoder (125 values):

    Rows 0–24: My pattern line fills
    Rows 25–49: My wall
    Rows 50–74: Opponent pattern line fills
    Rows 75–99: Opponent wall
    Rows 100–109: Scores, penalties, first-player token
    Rows 110–124: Tile availability

Proposed additions (~15–20 new values):

Original 125 + new features:

125–129 (5 values): My pattern line "cleanness" per row
  - Row r cleanness = 1.0 if wall[r] doesn't have the tile color yet, else 0.0
  - This encodes "is this pattern line safe to start / will it conflict?"
  - Example: if wall row 2 already has BLUE, then pattern line 2 is "dirty"

130–134 (5 values): Opponent's pattern line cleanness (same logic)

135–139 (5 values): My pattern line "fill ratio" per row
  - Row r fill ratio = (tiles on pattern line) / (row capacity r+1)
  - Example: row 0 capacity=1, row 4 capacity=5
  - Tells the net: which rows are closest to scoring

140–144 (5 values): Opponent's pattern line fill ratio

145: My first-player token value
  - 1.0 if I hold it (going last this round, get floor next round)
  - 0.0 if I don't
  - Heuristic: first-player token is valuable mid-game (controls tempo), 
    less valuable late game (fewer tiles left to exploit)

146: Opponent holds first-player token

147–151 (5 values): "Wall row completion" for me
  - Row r: how many tiles in the wall row are already placed (0–5)
  - Normalized: count / 5.0
  - Tells the net: which wall rows are close to full horizontal bonuses

152–156 (5 values): Opponent's wall row completion

157–161 (5 values): "Wall column completion" for me
  - Col c: how many tiles in the wall column are already placed (0–5)
  - Normalized: count / 5.0
  - Tells the net: vertical wall progress

162–166 (5 values): Opponent's wall column completion

167: "Board density" for me
  - (Total tiles in wall) / 25
  - Encodes: how far into the game are we
  - Also captures: later = fewer opportunities for new patterns, value of keeping lines clean

168: Opponent's board density

Total new: 44 values
Total encoding size: 125 + 44 = 169 values

Not huge, and all of it has clear chess-like logic that AB could benefit from if it wanted to tweak its hand-coded eval.
Implementation sketch

Sanity check: Run AB's hand-coded eval on a position, then ask: "Would AB benefit from knowing pattern line cleanness?" Answer: probably yes — it might avoid starting dirty lines. Same for wall completion (row bonuses). This validates the features.
Next steps

    Implement the encoder changes above. Add the 44 new features, run the test.
    Update AzulValueNet to accept input size 169 (currently hardcoded to 125).
    Benchmark AB at depth 3 and 4 on a fresh game to confirm wall time.
    Generate supervision data (10k examples at depth 3 or 4, whichever is fast enough).
    Train gen-0 value net on this data.
    Evaluate gen-0 vs hand-coded AB — should see ~55–60% win rate if the net learned anything.

Game positions. Azul can go more than 5 rounds but in quality games usually only go 5 rounds. When the final round is complete the value is the game result, no estimation needed. While the search would take a long time it’s might just be possible to do a full AB search of the final round. As such we are less worried about positional values with 1 turn to go because we can get final values easily. What we really want to know is the value of various end of round states before the next round fills the factories. AB should only search to the end of a round. Because you don’t know with the random factory draw will be to start the new round.
For training purposes we might want to take a position say 3-4 moves before then end of round 4 and determine the possible end of round states. Then play out those states using AB to see what the actual outcome was starting from each. That would give it more signal as to having a clear line is worth x or having the first player tile is worth y. 
So there might be some targeted positions  but there might also just be some played out games. 
Another thought is that as AB (or in this case maybe minimax is better to use) develops a search tree in the final round. It knows the final value of all possible states that reach their terminal states. Those become facts. At that point why play the game out you just record the minimax tree.

Q0.1: Feature Engineering — Comprehensive Brainstorm

Before we lock in the encoder, let's build a master list of candidate features. I'll organize them by category and we'll evaluate each.

    Observations from watching AB vs AB games that made you think "the eval is missing X"?

While you gather those, let me propose a starter list organized by strategic concept:
A. Pattern Line Health (Cleanness & Efficiency)

    Cleanness per row (already in my sketch) — is the wall color already placed?
    Fill ratio per row (already in my sketch) — how close to scoring?
    Overfill penalty indicator — if a row has 5 tiles on pattern line and we play another tile there, it goes to floor. Binary flag or count of "at-risk" rows?
    Wasted tile risk — e.g., pattern line row 4 has 4/5 tiles of BLUE; if the 5th BLUE tile will make a duplicate on wall, that row is "trapped". Measure: how many pattern lines can't possibly score because the color is blocked in the wall?

B. Wall Geometry & Bonuses

    Row completion ratio (already in my sketch) — progress toward row bonus
    Column completion ratio (already in my sketch) — progress toward column bonus
    5-in-a-row adjacency — how many rows have 4/5 tiles filled AND are near a completed 5-in-a-row (worth +10 points)?
    Diagonal clustering — are my completed tiles forming clusters (implying future adjacency bonuses) or scattered?

C. First-Player Tile & Round Dynamics

    First-player token ownership (already in my sketch)
    End-of-round indicator — is this the last move of the round? (Affects strategy: might trash to keep clean vs. force placement)
    Remaining moves this round — rough estimate of how many turns are left before factories empty

D. Tile Availability & Factory Structure

    Rarity of target color — if I want BLUE, how many BLUE tiles exist in all sources vs. total tiles? (Scarcity = harder to build this line)
    Factory concentration — are tiles clumped in 1–2 factories or spread? (Clumped = harder to pivot)
    Penalty-tile exposure — how many colors are "tainted" (only available in bad quantities, forcing floor moves)?

E. Scoring Positions & Momentum

    Score differential momentum — is my score accelerating or decelerating vs. opponent's? (e.g., last 2 rounds I gained +20, opponent gained +5, I'm pulling away)
    Penalty trajectory — am I accumulating floor penalties faster than opponent? (Early warning of doom)
    End-game bonus potential — how close am I to triggering the final-round rule? (Affects urgency)

F. Center vs. Factory Bias

    Center pile size — larger center = more chance for opportunistic grabs; smaller = fewer options
    Center tile diversity — is center a garbage pile or does it have useful tiles?
    Factory distribution by player — some factories are "mine" (I have good moves) vs. "theirs" (they have good moves)

You said: Board completion signals

Board completion signals
    •    Row completion ratios (5 values) — fraction of each wall row filled
    •    Column completion ratios (5 values) — fraction of each wall column filled
    •    Color completion ratios (5 values) — fraction of each color’s wall slots filled
    •    Total wall completion ratio (1 value) — overall fraction filled
Pattern line state
    •    Count of pattern lines that will be empty at start of next round (1 value) — lines that are either already empty or won’t complete this round
    •    Adjacency points grid (5×5 = 25 values) — for each empty wall slot, how many points placing there would earn given current neighbors
Flexibility / capacity planning
    •    Maximum placeable tiles per color next round (5 values) — sum of remaining capacity across all rows that can legally accept that color, given wall completions and pattern line occupations
    •    Maximum tiles total next round (1 value) — sum across all 5 colors

Some things I’ve noticed watch AZ and AB replays. Occasionally AZ would trash a tile that at the end of a round that would complete a partial line or that could just fill a complete line by itself. In these cases even though adjacency might be terrible it is simply the better move it scores 1 and avoids the floor. Though I think that was more AZ than AB. Both bots have a habit of starting rows but not completing them. Sometimes with the same colors. Ie it might have a partial row of blue and it might start a second partial row of blue. It is occasionally the best move but it’s rare. 
In the early stages of the game taking a higher quantity of tiles is often a good thing. At 5 rounds there are only 100 tiles in the game. Tiles go to the wall and score points. If you can get 60 tiles over the course of the game that means your opponent only got 40. That is a major benefit. While good adjacency strategy works in final rounds. Early game is more about acquiring as many tiles as possible. Alongside with the row and column completions the most important keys to winning are. Complete the first 2 rows and complete one column. So you almost have to pick a target column in the first few turns and focus on completing that. Use filler turns/tiles to complete the top 2 rows as you do. After that it’s about any additional adjacencies you can get and any defensive moves to block your opponent. Getting your target columns row 4 and 5 completed early give you space to place tiles you are grabbing for blocking purposes to avoid having to floor them. You often only complete 1 row 5 wall tile. The rest is “free trash storage”. The 10 point bonus usually only comes into play when your opponent is blocking your column and sleeps on the color bonus until it’s too late. 
And column over color importance makes sense also because of adjacency. I column is 5 adjacent by definition a color has 5 tiles none are adjacent with each other you need a second tile in that row to get adjacency

Claude responded: This is gold.

This is gold. You've just articulated the strategic hierarchy of Azul, and it's very different from what a naive eval might think. Let me consolidate this into a revised feature list that prioritizes what actually matters.
Strategic Hierarchy (from your insights)

    Column completion >> Row completion >> Color completion
        Columns guarantee adjacency (5 auto-adjacent tiles)
        Rows only give 10 bonus, don't help adjacency
        Colors are isolated unless you have multiple in same row
    Early game (rounds 1–3): Tile acquisition volume
        Getting 60 tiles vs opponent's 40 is the game
        Quantity > quality in early rounds
        Starting multiple partial lines of the same color is usually bad (wasteful capacity)
    Mid game (rounds 4–5): Row completion + column focus
        Complete rows 1–2 (cheap, easy points)
        Lock in target column early, defend it
        Complete row 5 in target column for "free trash storage"
    Late game (round 5+): Defensive blocking + adjacency optimization
        Opponent blocks your column? Don't panic, use bonus color completion as secondary
        Trash storage lets you avoid floor penalties
        10-point row bonus less important; adjacency is the game
    Round boundaries: End-of-round value is clearer than mid-round
        You're right: search to round end (factories empty), not into next round
        Value function needs to understand "keeping a row clean for next round" is worth -1 floor penalty

Revised Feature List (Priority-ordered)

I'm going to propose 64 new features (125 + 64 = 189 total). This is larger, but each feature has clear strategic meaning.
Tier 1: Column Completion (Top Priority)

  130–134: Opponent column completion ratios (5 values)

  140–144: Opponent column distance to completion

  160–164: Opponent pattern line cleanness
  165–169: My pattern line fill ratio (tiles placed / capacity)

  180–184: Opponent pattern line "will be empty next round"

Tier 4: Color Completion (Lower Priority)

Indices 185–189 (5 values):
  185–189: My color completion ratios (5 values, one per color)
  190–194: Opponent color completion ratios (5 values)

Rationale: Colors matter, but less than columns/rows. Still useful for long-term planning.
Tier 5: Tile Availability & Acquisition (Early-game signal)

Indices 195–199 (5 values):
  195–199: Tiles of each color remaining in game (count / 20, normalized)

Rationale: Early game: more tiles available = more grabs possible. Late game: scarcity affects what's feasible.

Indices 200–204 (5 values):
  200–204: Maximum placeable tiles per color next round (capacity / total_capacity_possible)

Rationale: This tells the net: "If I focus on BLUE next round, I can place up to 3 tiles" vs. "Only 1 slot available". Drives acquisition decisions.
Tier 6: First-Player Tile & Round Dynamics

Index 205 (1 value): I hold first-player token (1.0 / 0.0)
Index 206 (1 value): Opponent holds first-player token
Index 207 (1 value): Estimated moves remaining this round (remaining_tiles / legal_moves_count, clamped to [0,1])

Rationale: First-player token controls tempo. Moves-remaining affects urgency (trash to clean vs. commit).
Tier 7: Adjacency Opportunity Grid (Optional, higher computation)

Indices 208–232 (25 values):
  For each empty wall slot (5×5 grid):
    - Adjacency points if we place there now / max_possible_adjacency
    - Shows "good slots" vs. "dead slots"

Rationale: This directly tells the net where placements will compound value. But it's expensive to compute (requires looking at neighbors). Include only if we have cycles to spare.
Tier 8: Duplicate Starting Lines (Early-game anti-pattern)

Indices 233–237 (5 values):
  For each color:
    - Count of pattern lines currently containing that color / 5
    - 0.0 = good (no duplicate), 1.0 = all 5 rows have this color (bad)

Rationale: You noted this: starting the same color multiple times is waste. Flag it explicitly.
Tier 9: End-of-Game Bonus & Completion Proximity

Index 238 (1 value): Any player has triggered end-of-game condition (has_triggered_game_end as 1.0 / 0.0)
Index 239 (1 value): Rounds completed so far (round_number / 6, clamped)

Rationale: Late-game context. If someone triggered game-end, strategy shifts (fewer rounds left).

Q0.1d: Pattern Line Cleanness — Simpler or Exact?

You said cleanness is "more subtle." Here's what I proposed:

Cleanness per row = 1.0 if wall[row][wall_color] == 0 (color not placed)
                  = 0.0 if wall[row][wall_color] == 1 (color placed, this row can't score)

Is this the right signal, or should I compute it differently?

Example: If row 4 is empty on the wall but has 3 BLACK tiles on the pattern line, and wall[4][BLACK] == 0:

    Cleanness[4] = 1.0 (it's safe to place BLACK here)

But if wall[4][BLACK] == 1 (BLACK is already in row 4's wall slot):

    Cleanness[4] = 0.0 (can't place BLACK, row is blocked)

Is that right?

Claude responded: Perfect.

Perfect. I'm going to capture these as open items in the project plan — implementation details TBD when you have the bigger screen.

Let me add them to the brainstorm list:
Additional Features (Open Implementation)
Color Demand (Strategic Bottleneck Detection)

Concept: For each color, measure "demand pressure"
- How many rows/columns (mine + opponent's) need this color?
- How many tiles of this color are actually available?
- Ratio: demand / supply = urgency signal

Example:
  - BLUE: I need 2 (row 5 col 2), opponent needs 3 (col 3 + row 4)
  - Available BLUE: 4 tiles remaining
  - Demand = 5, Supply = 4 → BLUE is a bottleneck, grab it now
  
- Possibly per color: demand_count / available_count, clamped [0, 1]
  - 0.0 = oversupplied (no competition)
  - 1.0+ = undersupplied (hotly contested)

Open questions:

    How to weight "mine" vs "opponent's" demand? (Equal? Weighted toward opponent threat?)
    Include only incomplete patterns, or also count "could complete this round"?
    Store as 5 values (one per color) or something more complex?

Rows 1&2 Completion Feasibility (This Round)

Concept: Can I still complete rows 1 and 2 this round?
- Row 1 needs 1 tile, Row 2 needs 2 tiles of the matching color
- Check: do I have capacity (pattern line space) and is color available?

Indices: 2 values (one per row)
  - 1.0 = can complete this round (color available + capacity exists)
  - 0.5 = maybe (color might come out, but not guaranteed)
  - 0.0 = can't complete (color gone or no capacity)

Open questions:

    Feasibility based on "guaranteed to appear" vs "might appear"?
    Do we trust tile_availability() or do we need a "could still be in bag" estimate?

First-Player Tile Context Sensitivity

Current: Simple binary (I hold it / 0.0, opponent holds it / 0.0)

Refined:
- First-player value varies by round
  - Round 1–4: valuable (controls next round's tempo)
  - Round 5 (final): **negative value** (liability, causes extra floor penalty, no next round to benefit from tempo)
  - Round 6+ (overflow): even more negative

Possibly:
  - Add a "round number" feature (already proposed: Index 204?)
  - Let the net learn that first-player value flips sign in final round
  - OR: Encode as "first-player value coefficient" that's positive early, zero/negative late

Alternative simpler approach:
  - Store: (I hold FP) × (1.0 if round < 5, -0.5 if round == 5, -1.0 if round > 5)
  - Net learns: "in final round, avoid holding FP"

Open questions:

    Should this be baked into the feature, or let the model learn it?
    What's the actual penalty value of holding FP in round 5 vs round 1?

Factory Monocolor Events (Rare but Impactful)

Concept: "Does any factory have only one color (and that color is garbage)?"
- This affects first-player tile availability mid-round
- If a factory is all-one-color, the only move is to take that color (and lose the tile to center)
- Impacts round dynamics unexpectedly

Possibly:
  - Count of "monocolor factories" (1 value)
  - Average color concentration per factory (1 value)
  
This is probably low-priority, but worth noting.

Now, moving to Q0.2: Data Generation Strategy

While you get to the bigger screen to finalize encoder details, let's lock in how we generate training data.
Q0.2a: Data Volume & Depth Trade-off

You said:

    "Depth dependent on speed. This is a budget question where do we get the most bang for our buck."

My proposal:

Generate three datasets at different depths, see which teaches the net best:
Dataset	Depth	Approx. Positions	Wall Time	Quality
Small-shallow	2	5,000	~1 hour	Fast AB, noisy labels
Medium	3	10,000	~4 hours (overnight)	Balanced
Large-deeper	4	20,000	~12 hours (full day)	High-quality AB labels

Question: Which one should we try first for gen-0?

My recommendation: Start with Medium (depth 3, 10k examples)

    Reasonable wall time (4 hours)
    Strong signal (depth 3 is substantial)
    Can always retrain with depth 4 if gen-0 underperforms

Do you agree, or would you rather try one of the others?
Q0.2b: Position Sampling Strategy

You had a great insight:

    "AB should only search to the end of a round. Because you don't know with the random factory draw will be to start the new round."

And:

    "Targeted positions: take a position 3-4 moves before the end of round 4 and determine the possible end of round states. Then play out those states using AB to see what the actual outcome was."

Two sampling strategies:

Strategy A: Random mid-round positions

    Play a random game to a random mid-round position (move 2–8 of the round)
    Run AB depth 3 to find value
    Store (encoding, ab_value)
    Pros: Easy to generate, covers diverse positions
    Cons: Misses end-of-round clarity

Strategy B: End-of-round state projection (Your idea)

    Play random games to move 6–8 of round N (late in round)

    AB returns the complete tree with all node values

    Minimax: no pruning, guaranteed exhaustive
    AlphaBeta: pruning could skip some branches, but much faster

Q0.3b: Loss Function & Metrics

Regression loss:
python

loss_fn = nn.MSELoss()  # L2 loss: (pred - target)^2

This is standard for value estimation. The net learns to match AB's evaluations.

Additional metrics to track:
python

# Per epoch:
mae = mean_absolute_error(pred, target)  # Average prediction error in points/50
correlation = pearsonr(pred, target)     # How well predictions track targets

Why:

    MAE tells us: "On average, my predictions are off by ±X points"
    Correlation tells us: "Does my net track AB's relative rankings correctly?"
        If correlation = 0.95, the net learned the ranking; actual values might be scaled
        If correlation = 0.5, the net is confused; something's wrong

Question: Should we log these per epoch, or is just train/val loss enough?

My recommendation: Log train_loss, val_loss, val_mae, val_correlation per epoch.

    Tells us if the net is learning (loss drops), generalizing (val_loss not exploding), and ranking correctly (correlation high)

You said: I’m less familiar with what an epoch is can you explain it

I’m less familiar with what an epoch is can you explain it
2:16 PM
Claude responded: Great question.

Great question. Let me explain clearly:
What is an Epoch?

An epoch is one complete pass through your entire training dataset.
Example with your 10k supervision examples:

Training dataset: 10,000 (encoding, ab_value) pairs
Batch size: 128

One epoch = 10,000 / 128 = 78 batches

Epoch 1:
├─ Batch 1: train on examples 0–127
├─ Batch 2: train on examples 128–255
├─ Batch 3: train on examples 256–383
├─ ...
└─ Batch 78: train on examples 9,856–9,999
   (now we've seen all 10k examples once)

Epoch 2:
├─ Batch 1: train on examples 0–127 again (but net has changed since epoch 1)
├─ Batch 2: train on examples 128–255 again
├─ ...
└─ Batch 78: done with epoch 2

(repeat for epochs 3, 4, 5, ... until stopping criterion)

Why multiple epochs?

With only 10k examples, one pass through the data isn't enough for the net to learn the pattern. Think of it like studying:

    Reading a textbook once: you get something, but not mastery
    Reading it 3 times: you understand deeper
    Reading it 100 times: diminishing returns, probably memorizing

Similarly:

    1 epoch: net sees each example once, learns rough patterns
    10 epochs: net refines its understanding
    50 epochs: net has strong understanding
    200 epochs without early stopping: net starts memorizing noise (overfitting)

Training vs. Validation each epoch:

Epoch 42:
├─ Training phase:
│  ├─ Present all 78 batches of training data (8k examples)
│  ├─ Net makes predictions, compute loss, backprop, update weights
│  └─ Log: average training loss across all 78 batches
│
└─ Validation phase (after training phase):
   ├─ Present all validation data (2k examples, held-out)
   ├─ Net makes predictions, compute loss, but DON'T update weights
   ├─ Log: average validation loss
   └─ Check: if val_loss is better than any previous epoch, save checkpoint

Early Stopping (the key insight):

Epoch 1:  train_loss=0.50, val_loss=0.48  ✓ best val so far, save
Epoch 2:  train_loss=0.40, val_loss=0.42  ✓ best val so far, save
Epoch 3:  train_loss=0.35, val_loss=0.41  ✓ best val so far, save
Epoch 4:  train_loss=0.32, val_loss=0.43  ✗ worse than epoch 3
Epoch 5:  train_loss=0.30, val_loss=0.45  ✗ worse than epoch 3
...
Epoch 23: train_loss=0.05, val_loss=0.62  ✗ terrible (overfitting!)
          training_loss keeps dropping, but validation_loss is climbing
          this means: net is memorizing training data, not learning generalizable patterns

With early_stopping_patience=20:
├─ After epoch 3: best_val_loss = 0.41, patience_counter = 0
├─ After epoch 4: val_loss = 0.43 > 0.41, patience_counter = 1
├─ After epoch 5: val_loss = 0.45 > 0.41, patience_counter = 2
├─ ...
├─ After epoch 23: patience_counter = 20, STOP TRAINING
└─ Use the saved checkpoint from epoch 3 (best validation loss)

This prevents wasting time and prevents overfitting.
In your case:
python

train_value_net(
    train_loader,      # 8,000 examples in batches of 128
    val_loader,        # 2,000 examples in batches of 128
    max_epochs=200,    # Stop after 200 epochs at most
    early_stopping_patience=20,  # But also stop if val_loss doesn't improve for 20 epochs
)

Translation:

    We'll do at most 200 passes through the training data
    But if the validation loss stops improving for 20 consecutive epochs, we stop early and use the best checkpoint we found
    This usually happens around epoch 30–80 for supervised value learning (not 200)

Does this make sense? Any follow-up questions before we lock in Q0.3?

    Depth 3 for evaluation

    More rigorous test: does gen-0 net generalize to deeper search?
    But confounds the signal: is gen-0 losing because the net is bad, or because depth 4 is harder?

    Once we confirm learning works, we can test generalization to other depths later

Q0.4d: Generational Loop Structure

Once gen-0 succeeds (win rate > 52%), we move to gen-1:
python

for generation in range(max_generations):
    print(f"\n=== Generation {generation} ===")
    
    # Step 1: Generate supervision data
    if generation == 0:
        supervisor = AlphaBetaAgent(depth=3, threshold=0)  # Hand-coded eval
    else:
        supervisor = AlphaBetaWithLearnedValue(
            depth=3, 
            threshold=0,
            net_path=f"checkpoints/gen_{generation-1:04d}/best_checkpoint.pt"
        )
    
    print(f"Generating {target_examples} supervision examples...")
    train_data, val_data = generate_supervision_data(
        supervisor,
        num_examples=target_examples,
        threshold=5,  # Exhaustive search when <= 5 moves left
    )
    
    # Step 2: Train value net
    print(f"Training value net...")
    best_checkpoint = train_value_net(
        train_data,
        val_data,
        save_dir=f"checkpoints/gen_{generation:04d}/",
    )
    
    # Step 3: Evaluate
    print(f"Evaluating gen {generation}...")
    win_rate = evaluate_agents(
        AlphaBetaWithLearnedValue(depth=3, net_path=best_checkpoint),
        AlphaBetaAgent(depth=3),  # vs hand-coded baseline
        num_games=50,
    )
    print(f"Gen {generation} vs hand-coded: {win_rate}%")
    
    # Step 4: Decide: continue or stop?
    if generation > 0:
        prev_win_rate = win_rates[generation - 1]
        improvement = win_rate - prev_win_rate
        if improvement < 2%:
            print(f"Plateau detected (improvement: {improvement}%). Stopping.")
            break
    
    win_rates[generation] = win_rate

Questions:

    Max generations? Should we cap at gen-10, gen-20, or run until plateau?
        My recommendation: Cap at gen-10 (if not plateaued by then, something's wrong)
    Data per generation? Same 10k examples each time, or scale up?
        My recommendation: Gen-0 to Gen-2: 10k each (speed), Gen-3+: 15k–20k (refinement)
    Plateau threshold? I said < 2% improvement = stop. Does that sound right?
        Or should it be < 1%, < 5%?
        My recommendation: < 2% (conservative; we want real progress)

Q0.4f: Supervised Learning & When to Stop

Excellent question. Yes, this is supervised learning, which is different from RL.
Key difference:

Reinforcement Learning (AlphaZero style):

    Agent plays games, gets rewards, learns what leads to winning
    Needs to know: "When do we stop exploring new strategies?"
    Answer: when win rate plateaus, or we've learned enough

Supervised Learning (what we're doing):

    We have ground-truth labels (AB's values)
    Net learns to match those labels
    Natural stopping point: when the net can't get better at matching the labels

How supervised learning decides "we're done":

Epoch 1:  val_loss = 0.50  ← net is learning
Epoch 2:  val_loss = 0.45  ← still improving
Epoch 3:  val_loss = 0.42  ← still improving
...
Epoch 50: val_loss = 0.038 ← best yet
Epoch 51: val_loss = 0.039 ← worse (early stopping trigger)
Epoch 52: val_loss = 0.041 ← still worse
...
Epoch 70: val_loss = 0.050 ← plateau (early stopping stops us)

Result: Use epoch 50's checkpoint. We're done training.

This is automatic via early stopping.
But generational learning is different:

Once gen-0 is trained, we ask: "Does gen-0's improved evaluations help AB win more games?"

If yes (>55% win rate): gen-0 succeeded, generate better supervision with gen-0, train gen-1.
If no (<55% win rate): gen-0 failed, debug or stop.

So generational stopping is about: "Is the next generation worth training?"

Gen-0: vs hand-coded = 58% win rate ✓ success
       Generate new supervision using gen-0 net
Gen-1: vs hand-coded = 62% win rate ✓ success
       Generate new supervision using gen-1 net
Gen-2: vs hand-coded = 61% win rate ✗ regression or noise
       Still try gen-3 (might recover)
Gen-3: vs hand-coded = 62% win rate ✓ back on track
Gen-4: vs hand-coded = 62% win rate ✗ plateau
       Check if plateau is real (run more games?) or noise
Gen-5: vs hand-coded = 63% win rate ✓ tiny improvement
       Continue or stop? (tiny improvement might not be worth computation)

My recommendation for stopping rule:

Within generations:

    Early stopping (automatic, no manual decision needed)

Across generations:

    Stop if: win rate vs hand-coded plateaus for 2–3 consecutive generations
    OR: hit gen-10 (hard cap)
    OR: user hits Ctrl+C (graceful interrupt)

You asked: "Do we need to know when to stop?"

Answer: Not really. The system tells us when it's done via win rate plateau. We just watch the metrics and stop when diminishing returns appear.

Does this clarify the difference? Ready to move to Q0.5: UI Integration & Overall Project Phases?

Claude responded: Perfect.

Perfect. Let me write up a comprehensive new project plan based on everything we've locked in.
Azul AlphaZero → Value Net Learning — Project Plan

    Status: Phase redesign complete (pivot from AlphaZero to supervised value learning) Last updated: 2026-05-08 Goal: Build a superhuman Azul bot via learned value function + AlphaBeta search

Vision Shift

Original goal: AlphaZero-style self-play training → strong bot

Reality: Neural policy head stagnated (gen 1–2). MCTS distributions near-random. Training signal corrupted.

New approach: Abandon policy learning. Use supervised value learning instead.

    AlphaBeta search provides ground-truth position evaluation
    Train neural value net to match AB evaluations
    Integrate learned value into AB search
    Iterate: each generation's net produces better supervision for the next

Expected outcome: 90%+ win rate vs AB hard. Superhuman Azul play.
Architecture: AlphaBeta + Learned Value

Classical AlphaBeta search with two evaluation options:

Hand-Coded Eval (baseline):
  value = player.earned_differential (current score advantage)
  
Learned Value (gen-0+):
  encoding = encode_game(position)
  value = value_net(encoding)  [trained on AB supervision]
  
Integration:
  AlphaBetaWithLearnedValue(depth=3, threshold=8, net_path="gen_0000.pt")
    ├─ Searches to depth 3 or until round boundary
    ├─ Uses net prediction at leaf nodes (instead of hand-coded eval)
    └─ Returns best move

Why this works:

    AlphaBeta is fast, deterministic, correct
    Hand-coded eval has constant signal but limited strategic understanding
    Neural value net learns to refine evaluation (cleanness, column strategy, end-game dynamics)
    Generational improvement: gen-1 net sees better AB evaluations, produces better supervision for gen-2

Feature Engineering (OPEN — Requires Bigger Screen)
Locked-In Features

Tier 1: Column Completion (Strategic Priority)

    5 column completion ratios (per column, normalized)
    My max + 2nd-max column completion
    Opponent max + 2nd-max
    Rationale: Columns drive Azul scoring; 5 tiles auto-adjacent

Tier 2: Row Completion

    5 row completion ratios (per row, normalized by row capacity)
    My top-3 rows + opponent top-3
    Rationale: Rows 1–2 are cheap early-game; row bonuses are meaningful

Tier 3: Color Completion

    5 color completion ratios (per color)
    My max color + opponent max
    Rationale: Color bonuses sneak up; less critical than columns/rows

Tier 4: Pattern Line Health

    Pattern line cleanness per row (1.0 if wall color not yet placed, 0.0 if blocked)
    Pattern line fill ratio per row (current tiles / capacity)
    Pattern lines that will be empty next round (1.0 if won't complete)
    Total tiles on pattern lines / 15 (capacity crunch indicator)
    Rationale: Knowing which lines are "safe" vs "trapped" vs "overcommitted" is crucial

Tier 5: Pattern Line Flexibility

    Count of incomplete pattern lines per color (flags multi-line risk)
    Rationale: Starting the same color twice is usually wasteful; flags trap states

Tier 6: First-Player Tile & Game End

    I hold first-player token / opponent holds
    Can I end the game this round? / Can opponent?
    Rounds remaining for me / opponent
    Rationale: First-player controls tempo; end-game control is a huge advantage

Tier 7: Tile Availability

    Tiles remaining by color / 20
    Source counts by color / 5
    Rationale: Scarcity affects acquisition decisions

Open Implementation (Deferred)

Color Demand / Bottleneck Detection

    For each color: measure demand (how many rows/columns need it) vs supply
    Flag contested colors (demand > supply)
    Implementation TBD

Row 1&2 Source Concentration

    For each of rows 1 and 2: count factories with required color
    If only 1 factory has it: urgency flag
    If 2+ factories: low urgency
    Implementation TBD

First-Player Round Context

    Value of first-player varies by round (positive early, negative in final round)
    May need round-aware coefficient
    Implementation TBD

Adjacency Grid (Optional, Expensive)

    For each empty wall slot: adjacency points if placed there
    25 values for full grid
    Deferred: expensive to compute, may not be needed for gen-0

Final Encoder Size: 169–189 values (TBD after implementing locked features)
Data Generation Strategy
Phase 0: Supervision Data (Days 1–2)

Algorithm: Game playthrough with exhaustive search at round boundaries
python

Key points:

    Play full random games to natural termination
    When <= 5 moves remain in a round: exhaustive AB search (depth=999, threshold=0)
    AB's pruning is trusted: if a branch is pruned, assume generalization will also skip it
    Collect all visited nodes from the AB tree
    Transitive benefit: depth-4 search implicitly gives us depth-3, depth-2, depth-1 data

Wall time estimate:

    5k examples: 1–2 hours
    10k examples: 4–6 hours (overnight acceptable)
    20k examples: 12+ hours (full day)

Smoke test approach: Start with 5k–10k, evaluate win rate. Scale up for later generations if needed.
Training Pipeline: Value Net
Phase 1: Supervised Value Learning (Days 2–4)

Hyperparameters:

    Input size: 169 (encoder size, TBD)
    Hidden dim: 64 (small, effective)
    Dropout: 0.1 (mild regularization)
    Learning rate: 1e-3 (standard for supervised regression)
    Batch size: 128
    Train/val split: 80/20
    Max epochs: 200
    Early stopping patience: 20 epochs

Logging (per epoch):

    train_loss, val_loss, val_mae, val_correlation, learning_rate
    Save to CSV: checkpoints/gen_NNNN/training_log.csv

Checkpointing:

    Save best checkpoint at lowest val_loss
    Save final summary: summary.txt (best_epoch, best_val_loss, final_metrics, wall_time)

Visualization (post-training):

    loss_curves.png — train vs val loss over epochs
    metrics_curves.png — val MAE + val correlation
    All generated by matplotlib, saved to checkpoints/gen_NNNN/

Notes for Implementation

    Generational plateau is normal — Don't expect linear improvement forever. Each generation compounds on the last; diminishing returns kick in fast.
    Keyboard interrupt gracefully — Allow the user to stop training mid-loop with Ctrl+C. Save results, don't crash.

You said: When we create training games are the games stored in ram or to disk?

When we create training games are the games stored in ram or to disk?
2:38 PM
Claude responded: Great question.

Great question. Let me think through the options:
Current Approach (What I Sketched)

In generate_ab_supervision.py, I showed:
python

training_data = []  # List in RAM

for game_idx in range(num_games):
    game = Game(seed=random())
    
    while not game.is_round_over():
        # ... play moves ...
        
        if remaining_moves <= threshold:
            ab_tree = ab.build_complete_tree(game)
            for node in ab_tree.all_nodes():
                encoding = encode_game(node.state)
                value = node.value
                training_data.append((encoding, value))  # ← Added to RAM list

This is RAM-only. Everything stays in memory until the function returns.
Issues with RAM-only approach

For 10k examples:

    Each example: encoding (169 floats @ 4 bytes = 676 bytes) + value (1 float @ 4 bytes = 4 bytes) = ~680 bytes
    10k examples × 680 bytes = ~6.8 MB
    Very manageable, no problem

For 100k examples:

    100k × 680 bytes = ~68 MB
    Still fine on consumer hardware

But as we scale (future):

    1M examples = 680 MB
    10M examples = 6.8 GB
    Now we have a problem on a machine with 16 GB RAM

Better Approach: Stream to Disk

I recommend hybrid: stream to disk, load in batches during training.
python

def generate_ab_supervision(supervisor_agent, num_examples, output_file="train_data.pt"):
    """Generate supervision and stream to disk."""
    
    examples_collected = 0
    encodings = []
    values = []
    
    for game_idx in range(num_games):
        game = Game(seed=random())
        
        while not game.is_game_over():
            while not game.is_round_over():
                moves = game.legal_moves()
                game.make_move(random.choice(moves))
                game.advance(skip_setup=True)
                
                remaining_moves = estimate_remaining_moves(game)
                if remaining_moves <= 5:
                    ab_tree = ab.build_complete_tree(game)
                    
                    for node in ab_tree.all_nodes():
                        if node.was_visited:
                            encoding = encode_game(node.state)
                            value = node.value
                            
                            encodings.append(encoding)
                            values.append(value)
                            examples_collected += 1
                            
                            # Flush to disk every 1000 examples (avoid RAM buildup)
                            if len(encodings) >= 1000:
                                _flush_to_disk(encodings, values, output_file, append=True)
                                encodings = []
                                values = []
                                print(f"Collected {examples_collected} examples...")
            
            game.advance()
    
    # Flush remaining
    if encodings:
        _flush_to_disk(encodings, values, output_file, append=True)
    
    print(f"Total: {examples_collected} examples saved to {output_file}")
    return output_file

def _flush_to_disk(encodings, values, output_file, append=False):
    """Save a batch of examples to disk."""
    encodings = torch.tensor(encodings, dtype=torch.float32)
    values = torch.tensor(values, dtype=torch.float32)
    
    if append and os.path.exists(output_file):
        # Load existing, append, save
        existing = torch.load(output_file)
        encodings = torch.cat([existing['encodings'], encodings], dim=0)
        values = torch.cat([existing['values'], values], dim=0)
    
    torch.save({
        'encodings': encodings,
        'values': values,
    }, output_file)

File size on disk:

    10k examples: ~6.8 MB (single .pt file)
    100k examples: ~68 MB
    1M examples: ~680 MB

All reasonable on modern systems.
Loading for Training

Then in train_value_net.py:
python

class SupervisionDataset(torch.utils.data.Dataset):
    def __init__(self, data_file):
        self.data = torch.load(data_file)
        self.encodings = self.data['encodings']
        self.values = self.data['values']
    
    def __len__(self):
        return len(self.encodings)
    
    def __getitem__(self, idx):
        return self.encodings[idx], self.values[idx]

# Usage
dataset = SupervisionDataset("train_data.pt")
train_set, val_set = random_split(dataset, [0.8, 0.2])
train_loader = DataLoader(train_set, batch_size=128, shuffle=True)
val_loader = DataLoader(val_set, batch_size=128)

The DataLoader loads examples into RAM in batches as needed during training. Efficient and scalable.
My Recommendation

Use hybrid approach:

    During generation: Stream to disk in batches (flush every 1000 examples)
        Avoids RAM buildup
        Easy to resume if interrupted (each flush is a checkpoint)
        Clear progress (can print "Collected X examples")
    During training: Load from disk via PyTorch DataLoader
        Automatic batching
        Shuffling for each epoch
        Memory-efficient (only one batch in RAM at a time)
    Storage: Single .pt file per generation
        checkpoints/gen_0000/train_data.pt
        ~7 MB for 10k examples
        Easy to inspect, delete, archive

Alternative: Just Keep in RAM (Simpler for now)

If we're targeting 10k examples for gen-0, honestly RAM-only is fine:

    10k examples = ~7 MB
    No disk I/O overhead
    Simpler code
    Faster generation

python

training_data = []
for ...:
    for node in ab_tree.all_nodes():
        training_data.append((encoding, value))

# Convert to tensors for training
encodings = torch.tensor([e for e, _ in training_data])
values = torch.tensor([v for _, v in training_data])

My call: Start simple (RAM-only for 10k), upgrade to disk streaming if we scale to 100k+ later.
Your call

Which approach do you prefer?

    RAM-only (simpler, fine for 10k examples)
    Stream to disk (more robust, scales to 100k+, slightly more complex)
    Hybrid (best of both: flush every 1000, load via DataLoader)

I'd lean toward #1 (RAM-only) for gen-0 smoke test, then upgrade to #3 (hybrid) for gen-1+ if we scale up.

What's your preference?

Excellent idea. A thin UI application for orchestrating the training pipeline would be really valuable. Let me sketch this out:
Training Dashboard UI (Thin Web App)
Architecture

Frontend (React or vanilla JS):
  ├─ Dashboard page
  │  ├─ Current status (idle / generating / training / evaluating)
  │  ├─ Live metrics (games collected, training loss, eval progress)
  │  ├─ Control buttons (Start Gen, Pause, Stop)
  │  └─ Generation history table
  │
  └─ Settings page
     ├─ Target examples per gen
     ├─ Depth parameters
     ├─ Training hyperparams (LR, batch size, etc.)
     └─ Save/Load configs

Backend (FastAPI):
  ├─ /status — current pipeline state
  ├─ /start_generation — begin data collection
  ├─ /stop_generation — pause/stop data collection
  ├─ /start_training — begin training on collected data
  ├─ /stop_training — pause/stop training
  ├─ /generations — list all completed gens with stats
  ├─ /generation/<id>/details — full stats for one gen
  ├─ /generation/<id>/plots — retrieve loss curves, metrics
  └─ /settings — GET/POST training config

Pipeline Orchestrator (Background Worker):
  ├─ Runs generation, training, eval in sequence
  ├─ Responds to start/stop/pause signals
  ├─ Writes live metrics to a state file or DB
  └─ Auto-proceeds to next gen if enabled

Questions Before We Design This
Q: Scope & Complexity

How much do you want the UI to do?

Option A: Minimal (watch-only)

    Display current status
    Show plots/stats
    Manual CLI triggers: python scripts/train_value_generations.py --gen 0
    UI is read-only

Option B: Light Control

    Start/stop buttons in UI
    Settings form for hyperparams
    But generational loop still runs sequentially (gen-0 completes, user clicks "start gen-1")

Option C: Fully Automated

    UI starts the pipeline
    Pipeline auto-proceeds through gens (gen-0 → train → eval → auto-start gen-1)
    UI is control + display
    More complex backend orchestration

My recommendation: Option B (light control) for MVP

    Gives you visibility and control
    Simpler backend (no complex async orchestration)
    Can upgrade to Option C later

Your preference?

    Add /training/* endpoints to api/main.py
    Single FastAPI app handles game + training dashboard
    Reuse existing HTML/JS infrastructure

Option B: Separate training dashboard

    New Flask or FastAPI app (port 8001)
    Game API stays at 8000
    Training dashboard at 8001

Q: Live Metrics Storage

How should we track live metrics as training happens?

Option A: File-based (simplest)

checkpoints/gen_0000/
├─ status.json          ← Current status ({"phase": "training", "epoch": 42, ...})
├─ training_log.csv    ← Per-epoch metrics

    API reads these files
    Frontend polls /status endpoint
    Simple, no database needed

Option B: In-memory state (faster)
python

class PipelineState:
    current_generation = 0
    phase = "idle"  # generating, training, evaluating
    games_collected = 0
    current_epoch = 0
    train_loss = 0.0
    # ... etc

    API holds state in memory
    Frontend polls for updates
    Faster, but lost if server restarts

Option C: SQLite (persistent + queryable)

    Overkill for this, probably
    But allows queries like "show me all gens where val_loss < 0.05"

My recommendation: Option A (file-based)

    Persistent across server restarts
    Simple to implement
    Readable for debugging
    Frontend doesn't need special knowledge

    Requires build step (npm run build)

    Consistent codebase
    No extra toolchain

    <script>
        // Poll interval (milliseconds)
        const POLL_INTERVAL = 2000;

        // Start polling on page load
        window.addEventListener('load', () => {
            pollStatus();
            setInterval(pollStatus, POLL_INTERVAL);
        });

        async function pollStatus() {
            try {
                const response = await fetch('/training/status');
                const data = await response.json();
                updateUI(data);
            } catch (error) {
                console.error('Error polling status:', error);
            }
        }

        function updateUI(data) {
            // Update status badge
            const badge = document.getElementById('status-badge');
            const statusText = document.getElementById('status-text');
            const phaseMap = {
                'idle': { class: 'status-idle', text: 'Idle' },
                'generating': { class: 'status-generating', text: 'Generating Data...' },
                'training': { class: 'status-training', text: 'Training...' },
                'evaluating': { class: 'status-evaluating', text: 'Evaluating...' },
            };
            const phaseInfo = phaseMap[data.phase] || phaseMap['idle'];
            badge.className = 'status-badge ' + phaseInfo.class;
            badge.textContent = phaseInfo.text;
            statusText.textContent = `Generation ${data.current_generation}`;

            // Update progress
            const progressFill = document.getElementById('progress-fill');
            const progressText = document.getElementById('progress-text');
            progressFill.style.width = (data.progress * 100) + '%';
            progressText.textContent = Math.round(data.progress * 100) + '%';

            // Update live metrics
            document.getElementById('metric-games').textContent = data.games_collected || 0;
            document.getElementById('metric-epoch').textContent = data.current_epoch !== undefined ? data.current_epoch : '—';
            document.getElementById('metric-train-loss').textContent = data.train_loss !== undefined ? data.train_loss.toFixed(4) : '—';
            document.getElementById('metric-val-loss').textContent = data.val_loss !== undefined ? data.val_loss.toFixed(4) : '—';
            document.getElementById('metric-correlation').textContent = data.val_correlation !== undefined ? data.val_correlation.toFixed(3) : '—';

            // Update button states
            document.getElementById('btn-start-gen').disabled = data.phase !== 'idle';
            document.getElementById('btn-stop-gen').disabled = data.phase !== 'generating';
            document.getElementById('btn-start-train').disabled = data.phase !== 'idle' || !data.data_ready;
            document.getElementById('btn-stop-train').disabled = data.phase !== 'training';
            document.getElementById('btn-start-eval').disabled = data.phase !== 'idle' || !data.model_ready;

            // Update generation table
            updateGenerationTable(data.generations);

            // Update plots
            if (data.latest_gen_plots) {
                const lossImg = document.getElementById('plot-loss');
                const metricsImg = document.getElementById('plot-metrics');
                if (data.latest_gen_plots.loss_curves) {
                    lossImg.src = data.latest_gen_plots.loss_curves + '?t=' + Date.now();
                    lossImg.style.display = 'block';
                }
                if (data.latest_gen_plots.metrics_curves) {
                    metricsImg.src = data.latest_gen_plots.metrics_curves + '?t=' + Date.now();
                    metricsImg.style.display = 'block';
                }
            }
        }

        function updateGenerationTable(generations) {
            const tbody = document.getElementById('gen-table-body');
            tbody.innerHTML = '';
            if (!generations) return;

            generations.forEach(gen => {
                const row = document.createElement('tr');
                row.innerHTML = `
                    <td>Gen ${gen.id}</td>
                    <td>${gen.status}</td>
                    <td>${gen.games_collected}</td>
                    <td>${gen.best_val_loss ? gen.best_val_loss.toFixed(4) : '—'}</td>
                    <td>${gen.win_rate_baseline ? (gen.win_rate_baseline * 100).toFixed(1) + '%' : '—'}</td>
                    <td>${gen.win_rate_hard ? (gen.win_rate_hard * 100).toFixed(1) + '%' : '—'}</td>
                    <td>${gen.wall_time || '—'}</td>
                `;
                tbody.appendChild(row);
            });
        }

        // Control functions
        async function startGeneration() {
            await fetch('/training/start_generation', { method: 'POST' });
            pollStatus();
        }

        async function stopGeneration() {
            await fetch('/training/stop_generation', { method: 'POST' });
            pollStatus();
        }

        async function startTraining() {
            await fetch('/training/start_training', { method: 'POST' });
            pollStatus();
        }

        async function stopTraining() {
            await fetch('/training/stop_training', { method: 'POST' });
            pollStatus();
        }

        async function startEvaluation() {
            await fetch('/training/start_evaluation', { method: 'POST' });
            pollStatus();
        }

        async function saveSettings() {
            const settings = {
                target_examples: parseInt(document.getElementById('setting-target-examples').value),
                threshold: parseInt(document.getElementById('setting-threshold').value),
                depth: parseInt(document.getElementById('setting-depth').value),
                learning_rate: parseFloat(document.getElementById('setting-lr').value),
                batch_size: parseInt(document.getElementById('setting-batch-size').value),
            };
            await fetch('/training/settings', {
                method: 'POST',
                headers: { 'Content-Type': 'application/json' },
                body: JSON.stringify(settings),
            });
            alert('Settings saved!');
        }
    </script>
</body>
</html>

Backend Spec: FastAPI endpoints in api/main.py
python

# New imports
from pydantic import BaseModel
import json
import os
from pathlib import Path

# Status schema
class TrainingStatus(BaseModel):
    phase: str  # "idle", "generating", "training", "evaluating"
    current_generation: int
    progress: float  # 0.0 to 1.0
    games_collected: int
    current_epoch: Optional[int]
    train_loss: Optional[float]
    val_loss: Optional[float]
    val_correlation: Optional[float]
    data_ready: bool
    model_ready: bool
    generations: List[Dict]
    latest_gen_plots: Optional[Dict]

# Settings schema
class TrainingSettings(BaseModel):
    target_examples: int = 10000
    threshold: int = 5
    depth: int = 3
    learning_rate: float = 1e-3
    batch_size: int = 128

# Global state (in-memory + backed by file)
class PipelineState:
    def __init__(self, state_file="checkpoints/pipeline_state.json"):
        self.state_file = state_file
        self.state = {
            'phase': 'idle',
            'current_generation': 0,
            'games_collected': 0,
            'current_epoch': None,
            'train_loss': None,
            'val_loss': None,
            'val_correlation': None,
        }
        self.load()
    
    def load(self):
        if os.path.exists(self.state_file):
            with open(self.state_file, 'r') as f:
                self.state = json.load(f)
    
    def save(self):
        os.makedirs(os.path.dirname(self.state_file), exist_ok=True)
        with open(self.state_file, 'w') as f:
            json.dump(self.state, f)
    
    def update(self, **kwargs):
        self.state.update(kwargs)
        self.save()

pipeline_state = PipelineState()

# Endpoints
@app.get("/training/status", response_model=TrainingStatus)
async def get_training_status():
    """Get current training pipeline status."""
    generations = []
    for gen_dir in sorted(Path("checkpoints").glob("gen_*")):
        gen_id = int(gen_dir.name.split("_")[1])
        summary_file = gen_dir / "summary.txt"
        summary = {}
        if summary_file.exists():
            # Parse summary.txt
            with open(summary_file, 'r') as f:
                lines = f.readlines()
                # (simple parsing; could be more robust)
                summary = {
                    'status': 'complete',
                    'games_collected': 0,
                    'best_val_loss': None,
                    'win_rate_baseline': None,
                    'win_rate_hard': None,
                    'wall_time': None,
                }
        generations.append({
            'id': gen_id,
            **summary,
        })
    
    latest_gen_plots = None
    if generations:
        latest_gen_dir = Path("checkpoints") / f"gen_{generations[-1]['id']:04d}"
        if (latest_gen_dir / "loss_curves.png").exists():
            latest_gen_plots = {
                'loss_curves': f"/training/generation/{generations[-1]['id']}/plot/loss_curves.png",
                'metrics_curves': f"/training/generation/{generations[-1]['id']}/plot/metrics_curves.png",
            }
    
    return TrainingStatus(
        phase=pipeline_state.state['phase'],
        current_generation=pipeline_state.state['current_generation'],
        progress=0.5,  # TBD: calculate from actual progress
        games_collected=pipeline_state.state['games_collected'],
        current_epoch=pipeline_state.state['current_epoch'],
        train_loss=pipeline_state.state['train_loss'],
        val_loss=pipeline_state.state['val_loss'],
        val_correlation=pipeline_state.state['val_correlation'],
        data_ready=False,  # TBD: check if training data exists
        model_ready=False,  # TBD: check if checkpoint exists
        generations=generations,
        latest_gen_plots=latest_gen_plots,
    )

@app.post("/training/start_generation")
async def start_generation():
    """Begin data generation for current generation."""
    pipeline_state.update(phase='generating', games_collected=0)
    # Trigger background task (TBD: use task queue or background worker)
    return {"status": "started"}

@app.post("/training/stop_generation")
async def stop_generation():
    """Stop data generation."""
    pipeline_state.update(phase='idle')
    return {"status": "stopped"}

@app.post("/training/start_training")
async def start_training():
    """Begin supervised training on collected data."""
    pipeline_state.update(phase='training', current_epoch=0)
    return {"status": "started"}

@app.post("/training/stop_training")
async def stop_training():
    """Stop training."""
    pipeline_state.update(phase='idle')
    return {"status": "stopped"}

@app.post("/training/start_evaluation")
async def start_evaluation():
    """Begin evaluation games."""
    pipeline_state.update(phase='evaluating')
    return {"status": "started"}

@app.get("/training/generation/{gen_id}/plot/{plot_name}")
async def get_generation_plot(gen_id: int, plot_name: str):
    """Serve plot image for a generation."""
    plot_path = Path(f"checkpoints/gen_{gen_id:04d}/{plot_name}")
    if plot_path.exists():
        return FileResponse(plot_path)
    return {"error": "Plot not found"}

@app.post("/training/settings")
async def save_training_settings(settings: TrainingSettings):
    """Save training settings."""
    with open("checkpoints/training_settings.json", 'w') as f:
        json.dump(settings.dict(), f)
    return {"status": "saved"}

@app.get("/training/settings")
async def get_training_settings():
    """Fetch current training settings."""
    if os.path.exists("checkpoints/training_settings.json"):
        with open("checkpoints/training_settings.json", 'r') as f:
            return json.load(f)
    return TrainingSettings().dict()

Orchestrator: scripts/training_dashboard_worker.py
python

#!/usr/bin/env python3
"""
Background worker for training pipeline orchestration.
Watches pipeline state and runs generation → training → eval loop.
"""

import json
import time
import subprocess
from pathlib import Path

STATE_FILE = "checkpoints/pipeline_state.json"

def load_state():
    if Path(STATE_FILE).exists():
        with open(STATE_FILE, 'r') as f:
            return json.load(f)
    return {'phase': 'idle', 'current_generation': 0}

def save_state(state):
    Path("checkpoints").mkdir(exist_ok=True)
    with open(STATE_FILE, 'w') as f:
        json.dump(state, f)

def run_generation(gen_id):
    """Run data generation for this generation."""
    print(f"\n=== Generation {gen_id} Data Collection ===")
    state = load_state()
    state['phase'] = 'generating'
    state['current_generation'] = gen_id
    state['games_collected'] = 0
    save_state(state)
    
    # Run data generation script
    result = subprocess.run([
        'python', '-m', 'scripts.generate_ab_supervision',
        f'--gen={gen_id}',
    ])
    
    if result.returncode == 0:
        print(f"Gen {gen_id} data collection complete.")
        state['phase'] = 'idle'
        save_state(state)
        return True
    else:
        print(f"Gen {gen_id} data collection failed.")
        return False

def run_training(gen_id):
    """Run supervised training for this generation."""
    print(f"\n=== Generation {gen_id} Training ===")
    state = load_state()
    state['phase'] = 'training'
    save_state(state)
    
    # Run training script
    result = subprocess.run([
        'python', '-m', 'scripts.train_value_net',
        f'--gen={gen_id}',
    ])
    
    if result.returncode == 0:
        print(f"Gen {gen_id} training complete.")
        state['phase'] = 'idle'
        save_state(state)
        return True
    else:
        print(f"Gen {gen_id} training failed.")
        return False

def run_evaluation(gen_id):
    """Run evaluation games for this generation."""
    print(f"\n=== Generation {gen_id} Evaluation ===")
    state = load_state()
    state['phase'] = 'evaluating'
    save_state(state)
    
    # Run evaluation script
    result = subprocess.run([
        'python', '-m', 'scripts.evaluate_agents',
        f'--gen={gen_id}',
    ])
    
    if result.returncode == 0:
        print(f"Gen {gen_id} evaluation complete.")
        state['phase'] = 'idle'
        save_state(state)
        return True
    else:
        print(f"Gen {gen_id} evaluation failed.")
        return False

def main():
    """Main orchestrator loop."""
    print("Training Dashboard Worker started. Watching for signals...")
    last_gen = -1
    
    while True:
        state = load_state()
        current_gen = state.get('current_generation', 0)
        phase = state.get('phase', 'idle')
        
        if phase == 'idle' and current_gen > last_gen:
            # New generation signal
            print(f"\nReceived signal to start generation {current_gen}")
            if run_generation(current_gen):
                last_gen = current_gen
        
        time.sleep(2)  # Poll every 2 seconds

if __name__ == '__main__':
    main()

How It All Works Together

    User opens dashboard → frontend/training_dashboard.html
    Dashboard polls → /training/status (every 2 seconds)
    API responds with current state (phase, metrics, generation history)
    User clicks "Start Generation" → POST /training/start_generation
    API updates state → pipeline_state.json (phase = 'generating')
    Background worker (training_dashboard_worker.py) watches state file
    Worker detects phase change → runs scripts/generate_ab_supervision.py
    Generation script updates metrics in real-time
    Generation completes → worker updates state (phase = 'idle')
    Dashboard re-polls → shows updated metrics, plots, generation table
    User clicks "Start Training" → repeat steps 4–10
