3:31 PM
Claude responded: Excellent questions.

Excellent questions. Let me think through both.
Question 1: Should 1 Always Be "Good" and 0 Always Be "Bad"?

Short answer: Yes, strongly recommend it.

Why:

    Neural net intuition: Networks learn that 1 = activate this feature, 0 = don't. If higher values mean "better for me," the gradient naturally flows toward amplifying that feature.
    Interpretability: When debugging, you can read the encoding and know "this player has high cleanness (0.9), low penalty (0.95 = clean), high column completion (0.8)" — all intuitively "good."
    Consistency: Makes feature engineering decisions transparent. You don't have to remember "oh, this one is inverted."

Current state (mostly good):

    ✓ Column completion: 1 = filled, 0 = empty (good = 1)
    ✓ Row completion: 1 = filled, 0 = empty (good = 1)
    ✓ Pattern fill: 1 = full, 0 = empty (good = 1)
    ✓ Cleanness: 1 = safe, 0 = blocked (good = 1)
    ✓ Floor penalty: 1 = clean, 0 = -14 (good = 1) ← already inverted
    ✓ Tiles available: 1 = many, 0 = none (good = 1)
    ✓ Scores: higher = better (good = 1)
    ✓ First-player token: 1 = I have it, 0 = I don't (good = 1)
    ✓ Can end game: 1 = I can, 0 = I can't (good = 1)

All features already align: 1 = good, 0 = bad.

Recommendation: Keep this convention throughout.
Question 2: Score Differential vs Both Scores

Current approach: Store both

encoding[idx] = my_score / 150.0
encoding[idx + 1] = opponent_score / 150.0

Alternative: Store differential

score_diff = (my_score - opponent_score) / 300.0  # [-1, 1] range, but we want [0, 1]
# Or normalize: (my_score - opponent_score + 150) / 300.0 → [0, 1]
encoding[idx] = (my_score - opponent_score + 150) / 300.0

Analysis:
Approach	Pros	Cons
Both scores	Net can compute whatever it wants (diff, ratio, etc.); doesn't assume linearity	Slightly redundant (net has to learn to subtract); uses 2 values
Score differential	Direct signal: "how much am I ahead"; net doesn't have to learn subtraction	Assumes linearity matters; loses individual context (both at 50 vs both at 150)

My take:

    Store both individual scores. The net is sophisticated enough to learn "I care about the difference," and it might also learn "absolute score matters" (e.g., in endgame, 150 vs 160 is tighter than 50 vs 60).
    Score differential is redundant. If the net needs it, it learns to compute it (linear combination of inputs). You're not saving much.
    Keep it simple: One feature = one value. Both scores = two values.

But this is a judgment call. What's your intuition?
Refined Feature List (With Scaling)

Here's the full encoding with consistent [0, 1] scaling and "1=good" convention:
python

def encode_game(game: Game) -> np.ndarray:
    """
    Encode game state as [0, 1] vector where 1 = good, 0 = bad.
    """
    encoding = np.zeros(101, dtype=np.float32)
    idx = 0
    
    # ========== Player Features (70) ==========
    encoding[idx:idx+70] = game.extract_features()
    # (Player.extract_features() returns [0,1] with 1=good)
    idx += 70
    
    # ========== Game-Level Features (6) ==========
    encoding[idx] = 1.0 if game.first_player_idx == 0 else 0.0  # 1=good for me
    encoding[idx + 1] = 1.0 if game.first_player_idx == 1 else 0.0  # 1=good for opponent
    encoding[idx + 2] = game._can_player_end_game(0)  # 1=good for me
    encoding[idx + 3] = game._can_player_end_game(1)  # 1=good for opponent
    encoding[idx + 4] = (6 - game.round_num) / 6.0  # 1=early game, 0=game ending (for me)
    encoding[idx + 5] = (6 - game.round_num) / 6.0  # same (padding or could omit)
    idx += 6
    
    # ========== Tile Availability (5) ==========
    availability = game.tile_availability()
    for color_idx in range(5):
        total_tiles = availability[color_idx][0]
        encoding[idx + color_idx] = total_tiles / 20.0  # 1=many, 0=none (good=1)
    idx += 5
    
    # ========== Source Counts (5) ==========
    for color_idx in range(5):
        source_count = availability[color_idx][1]
        encoding[idx + color_idx] = source_count / 5.0  # 1=spread, 0=concentrated (good=1)
    idx += 5
    
    # ========== Bag Contents (5) ==========
    bag_contents = [0] * 5
    for tile in game.bag:
        bag_contents[tile.tile] += 1
    for color_idx in range(5):
        encoding[idx + color_idx] = bag_contents[color_idx] / 20.0  # 1=plenty, 0=depleted (good=1)
    idx += 5
    
    # ========== Scores (5) ==========
    my_official = game.players[0].score
    my_earned = game.players[0].earned
    opp_official = game.players[1].score
    opp_earned = game.players[1].earned
    
    encoding[idx] = min(my_official / 150.0, 1.0)  # 1=high, 0=low (good=1)
    encoding[idx + 1] = min(my_earned / 150.0, 1.0)
    encoding[idx + 2] = min(opp_official / 150.0, 1.0)
    encoding[idx + 3] = min(opp_earned / 150.0, 1.0)
    encoding[idx + 4] = 0.0  # Padding
    idx += 5
    
    # ========== Floor Penalties (5) ==========
    my_penalty = game.players[0].penalty  # -14 to 0
    opp_penalty = game.players[1].penalty
    
    encoding[idx] = (my_penalty + 14) / 14.0  # 1=clean (0 penalty), 0=max penalty (-14) (good=1)
    encoding[idx + 1] = (opp_penalty + 14) / 14.0
    encoding[idx + 2] = 0.0
    encoding[idx + 3] = 0.0
    encoding[idx + 4] = 0.0
    idx += 5
    
    # ========== Bonus Points (5) ==========
    my_bonus = game.players[0].bonus
    opp_bonus = game.players[1].bonus
    
    encoding[idx] = min(my_bonus / 50.0, 1.0)  # 1=high bonus, 0=no bonus (good=1)
    encoding[idx + 1] = min(opp_bonus / 50.0, 1.0)
    encoding[idx + 2] = 0.0
    encoding[idx + 3] = 0.0
    encoding[idx + 4] = 0.0
    idx += 5
    
    assert idx == 101
    return encoding

Summary

Answer to both questions:

    Yes, 1 = good, 0 = bad for all features. Maintains consistency and neural net intuition.
    Store both scores, not differential. The net can learn differences, and individual scores carry useful signal.

Encoding size: 101 values, all [0, 1], all 1=good.

3:35 PM
Claude responded: Great catch.

Great catch. No, that's not safe. It's confusing.

If we're encoding from my perspective, then:

    My score high = good for me = 1
    Opponent score high = bad for me = 0

But if we encode opponent score as opponent_score / 150 directly, we get:

    Opponent score 150 = 1 (looks "good" by convention)
    Opponent score 0 = 0 (looks "bad")

This breaks the "1=good, 0=bad" convention for me.
Solution: Invert Opponent Scores
python

# ========== Scores (5) ==========
my_official = game.players[0].score
my_earned = game.players[0].earned
opp_official = game.players[1].score
opp_earned = game.players[1].earned

# My scores: 1=high (good for me)
encoding[idx] = min(my_official / 150.0, 1.0)
encoding[idx + 1] = min(my_earned / 150.0, 1.0)

# Opponent scores: INVERTED, so 1=low (good for me)
encoding[idx + 2] = 1.0 - min(opp_official / 150.0, 1.0)
encoding[idx + 3] = 1.0 - min(opp_earned / 150.0, 1.0)
encoding[idx + 4] = 0.0  # Padding

idx += 5

Now:

    My score 150 → 1.0 (I'm winning = good = 1) ✓
    Opponent score 150 → 0.0 (they're winning = bad for me = 0) ✓
    Opponent score 0 → 1.0 (they're losing = good for me = 1) ✓

Same logic applies to other "opponent" features:
python

# ========== Floor Penalties (5) ==========
my_penalty = game.players[0].penalty  # -14 to 0
opp_penalty = game.players[1].penalty

# My penalty: 1=clean, 0=penalized (1=good)
encoding[idx] = (my_penalty + 14) / 14.0

# Opponent penalty: INVERTED, so 1=penalized, 0=clean (1=good for me)
encoding[idx + 1] = 1.0 - ((opp_penalty + 14) / 14.0)

Now:

    I have -14 penalty → 0.0 (bad for me = 0) ✓
    Opponent has -14 penalty → 1.0 (good for me = 1) ✓

Updated Encoding
python

def encode_game(game: Game) -> np.ndarray:
    """
    Encode game state as [0, 1] vector where 1 = good FOR ME, 0 = bad FOR ME.
    """
    encoding = np.zeros(101, dtype=np.float32)
    idx = 0
    
    # ========== Player Features (70) ==========
    # Both players' features are from their own perspective
    encoding[idx:idx+70] = game.extract_features()
    idx += 70
    
    # ========== Game-Level Features (6) ==========
    encoding[idx] = 1.0 if game.first_player_idx == 0 else 0.0  # 1=good for me
    encoding[idx + 1] = 1.0 if game.first_player_idx == 1 else 0.0  # (redundant, but symmetric)
    encoding[idx + 2] = game._can_player_end_game(0)  # 1=good for me
    encoding[idx + 3] = game._can_player_end_game(1)  # (redundant, but symmetric)
    encoding[idx + 4] = (6 - game.round_num) / 6.0
    encoding[idx + 5] = (6 - game.round_num) / 6.0
    idx += 6
    
    # ========== Tile Availability (5) ==========
    # Neutral from both perspectives (more tiles is good for both)
    availability = game.tile_availability()
    for color_idx in range(5):
        encoding[idx + color_idx] = availability[color_idx][0] / 20.0
    idx += 5
    
    # ========== Source Counts (5) ==========
    for color_idx in range(5):
        encoding[idx + color_idx] = availability[color_idx][1] / 5.0
    idx += 5
    
    # ========== Bag Contents (5) ==========
    bag_contents = [0] * 5
    for tile in game.bag:
        bag_contents[tile.tile] += 1
    for color_idx in range(5):
        encoding[idx + color_idx] = bag_contents[color_idx] / 20.0
    idx += 5
    
    # ========== Scores (5) ==========
    my_official = game.players[0].score
    my_earned = game.players[0].earned
    opp_official = game.players[1].score
    opp_earned = game.players[1].earned
    
    # My scores: 1=high (good for me)
    encoding[idx] = min(my_official / 150.0, 1.0)
    encoding[idx + 1] = min(my_earned / 150.0, 1.0)
    
    # Opponent scores: INVERTED, 1=low (good for me)
    encoding[idx + 2] = 1.0 - min(opp_official / 150.0, 1.0)
    encoding[idx + 3] = 1.0 - min(opp_earned / 150.0, 1.0)
    encoding[idx + 4] = 0.0
    idx += 5
    
    # ========== Floor Penalties (5) ==========
    my_penalty = game.players[0].penalty  # -14 to 0
    opp_penalty = game.players[1].penalty
    
    # My penalty: 1=clean (good for me)
    encoding[idx] = (my_penalty + 14) / 14.0
    
    # Opponent penalty: INVERTED, 1=penalized (good for me)
    encoding[idx + 1] = 1.0 - ((opp_penalty + 14) / 14.0)
    encoding[idx + 2] = 0.0
    encoding[idx + 3] = 0.0
    encoding[idx + 4] = 0.0
    idx += 5
    
    # ========== Bonus Points (5) ==========
    my_bonus = game.players[0].bonus
    opp_bonus = game.players[1].bonus
    
    # My bonus: 1=high (good for me)
    encoding[idx] = min(my_bonus / 50.0, 1.0)
    
    # Opponent bonus: INVERTED, 1=low (good for me)
    encoding[idx + 1] = 1.0 - min(opp_bonus / 50.0, 1.0)
    encoding[idx + 2] = 0.0
    encoding[idx + 3] = 0.0
    encoding[idx + 4] = 0.0
    idx += 5
    
    assert idx == 101
    return encoding

Summary

Invert opponent-perspective features:

    Opponent scores: 1 - (opp_score / 150)
    Opponent penalty: 1 - ((opp_penalty + 14) / 14)
    Opponent bonus: 1 - (opp_bonus / 50)

Keep symmetric features as-is:

    Tile availability (more tiles helps both)
    Rounds remaining (same for both)

Result: All 101 values follow the convention: 1 = good for me, 0 = bad for me.

Does this make sense?
You said: I guess the question here is.

I guess the question here is. Is it still easy for the model to learn that opponent 0 is good and opponent 1 is bad.  If it’s just for human reading purpose I’d rather not invert opponent.
3:37 PM
Claude responded: Excellent point.

Excellent point. The model doesn't care about human readability—it cares about signal.

Let me think through this:

If we DON'T invert opponent features:

Opponent score 150 → 1.0
Opponent score 0 → 0.0

Can the model learn "opponent high = bad for me"?

Yes, absolutely. The model will learn:

    My score high (1.0) correlates with winning → weight positive
    Opponent score high (1.0) correlates with losing → weight negative

The network sees:

[my_score=0.9, opp_score=0.1] → I'm winning (good outcome)
[my_score=0.2, opp_score=0.8] → I'm losing (bad outcome)

Gradient learns: my_score weight ≈ +1, opp_score weight ≈ -1

This is trivial for a neural net. It's literally just learning a linear combination. The network will easily discover that opponent's high values are bad.

Comparison:

    Inverted: Network sees opponent score and can use it directly (weights ≈ +1)
    Not inverted: Network sees opponent score and negates it (weights ≈ -1)

Computational difference: Negligible. One extra subtraction per forward pass.

For human readability: Not inverted is much clearer. You can read the encoding and understand it directly.
My Recommendation

Don't invert opponent features.

Store them naturally:
python

# ========== Scores (5) ==========
encoding[idx] = min(game.players[0].score / 150.0, 1.0)      # My score
encoding[idx + 1] = min(game.players[0].earned / 150.0, 1.0) # My earned
encoding[idx + 2] = min(game.players[1].score / 150.0, 1.0)  # Opponent score (NOT inverted)
encoding[idx + 3] = min(game.players[1].earned / 150.0, 1.0) # Opponent earned (NOT inverted)
encoding[idx + 4] = 0.0

# ========== Floor Penalties (5) ==========
encoding[idx] = (game.players[0].penalty + 14) / 14.0        # My penalty
encoding[idx + 1] = (game.players[1].penalty + 14) / 14.0    # Opponent penalty (NOT inverted)

# ========== Bonus Points (5) ==========
encoding[idx] = min(game.players[0].bonus / 50.0, 1.0)       # My bonus
encoding[idx + 1] = min(game.players[1].bonus / 50.0, 1.0)   # Opponent bonus (NOT inverted)

Why:

    Network learns negation trivially (single weight)
    Human-readable (can inspect encoding directly)
    Simpler code (no inversions)
    No signal loss

The "1=good, 0=bad" convention is nice for us, but the network doesn't need it. It will learn whatever linear combination makes sense.

Agree?
You said: Ok good just confirming that relu doesn’t harm negative weights

Ok good just confirming that relu doesn’t harm negative weights
3:38 PM
Claude responded: Good question.

Good question. Let me clarify—ReLU doesn't prevent negative weights, but it does affect how they're used.
How ReLU Works with Negative Weights
python

# ReLU activation: f(x) = max(0, x)

# Example: encoding value = 0.8, weight = -2.0
z = 0.8 * (-2.0) = -1.6
output = ReLU(-1.6) = max(0, -1.6) = 0.0  ← Gets killed by ReLU

# Example: encoding value = 0.8, weight = +2.0
z = 0.8 * (+2.0) = 1.6
output = ReLU(1.6) = max(0, 1.6) = 1.6  ← Passes through

The key issue:

    Positive weights: Encoding value flows through, gets amplified or dampened
    Negative weights: Encoding value gets flipped negative, then ReLU kills it (sets to 0)

So negative weights can still exist and be learned, but they have a specific effect:

    If a feature value is high and you want to suppress it, use a negative weight
    ReLU will zero it out if the product is negative

For Your Opponent Score Example
python

# Opponent score: [0.0, 1.0] range (not inverted)

# Network learns (after backprop):
# Layer 1: opponent_score_weight ≈ -5.0 (negative!)

# Forward pass:
z = 0.8 * (-5.0) = -4.0
output = ReLU(-4.0) = 0.0  ← Gets killed

# Vs if we inverted:
z = 0.2 * (+5.0) = 1.0  ← Passes through, no ReLU kill
output = ReLU(1.0) = 1.0

The problem: With negative weights, high opponent scores get forced to zero by ReLU, losing expressiveness in the hidden layer.
Better Answer: Your Intuition Was Right

Actually, for the network to work well, you probably DO want to invert opponent features.

Why:

    Opponent score high (opponent winning) should map to low network activation (bad outcome)
    Without inversion: network has to learn negative weights + rely on ReLU killing them
    With inversion: network can use positive weights naturally

Simple fix:
python

# ========== Scores (5) ==========
encoding[idx] = min(game.players[0].score / 150.0, 1.0)
encoding[idx + 1] = min(game.players[0].earned / 150.0, 1.0)
encoding[idx + 2] = 1.0 - min(game.players[1].score / 150.0, 1.0)  # Inverted
encoding[idx + 3] = 1.0 - min(game.players[1].earned / 150.0, 1.0) # Inverted

Now:

    Opponent score 150 → 1.0 - 1.0 = 0.0 (low activation, bad outcome) ✓
    Opponent score 0 → 1.0 - 0.0 = 1.0 (high activation, good outcome) ✓

My Revised Recommendation

DO invert opponent features after all.

Not for human readability, but because:

    Network can use positive weights naturally
    Avoids relying on ReLU to kill negative products
    Better gradient flow
    Cleaner learned representations

Sorry for the flip-flop—your original caution was correct. ReLU + negative weights works, but it's suboptimal. Inversion is actually the better signal.

Agree?

3:41 PM
Claude responded: You're absolutely right.

You're absolutely right. That's the key insight I was missing.

The network doesn't care about individual scores in isolation—it cares about the relationship between them.
python

# What the network actually learns:
output = 0.9 * my_score - 0.9 * opp_score
       = 0.9 * (my_score - opp_score)
       = score_differential with a coefficient

# Or more generally:
output = w_mine * my_score + w_opp * opp_score
where w_opp is learned as negative

So storing both scores (not inverted) is actually perfect because:

    Network learns the differential naturally through its own weights
    It can learn other patterns too (e.g., "absolute score matters in endgame")
    The negative weight on opponent score is efficient—no ReLU killing anything if it's in a hidden layer

ReLU only matters if the raw input goes directly through ReLU. In a hidden layer:
python

# Layer 1:
z = w_my * my_score + w_opp * opp_score + bias
# w_opp will be learned as negative, z can be anything
# Then ReLU activates or not based on total z value, not individual weights

# Layer 2 can use the hidden output freely

Final Decision

Store opponent features naturally (not inverted):
python

# ========== Scores (5) ==========
encoding[idx] = min(game.players[0].score / 150.0, 1.0)
encoding[idx + 1] = min(game.players[0].earned / 150.0, 1.0)
encoding[idx + 2] = min(game.players[1].score / 150.0, 1.0)  # NOT inverted
encoding[idx + 3] = min(game.players[1].earned / 150.0, 1.0) # NOT inverted
encoding[idx + 4] = 0.0

# ========== Floor Penalties (5) ==========
encoding[idx] = (game.players[0].penalty + 14) / 14.0
encoding[idx + 1] = (game.players[1].penalty + 14) / 14.0  # NOT inverted

# ========== Bonus Points (5) ==========
encoding[idx] = min(game.players[0].bonus / 50.0, 1.0)
encoding[idx + 1] = min(game.players[1].bonus / 50.0, 1.0)  # NOT inverted

The network will learn +w * my_feature - w * opp_feature naturally through backprop. Clean, simple, efficient.

What happens if we don’t clamp a number to 1.0 and say pass it 1.10
3:47 PM
Claude responded: Good question.

Good question. What happens if we pass 1.10 to the network?

Nothing bad happens. The network will accept it just fine.
python

# If opponent earned = 165, and we do:
encoding[idx] = (165 + 14) / 150.0 = 179 / 150.0 = 1.193

# Network sees 1.193 as input
# It multiplies by weights, adds bias, applies ReLU/etc
# Completely normal operation

Clamping (using min(..., 1.0)) is optional:

    With clamp: All values guaranteed [0, 1], nice bounded input
    Without clamp: Values can exceed 1.0, but network still works fine

Tradeoffs:
Approach	Pros	Cons
With clamp	Bounded [0, 1], easier to reason about, matches "1=good" convention	Loses information if value exceeds 1 (rare in early game, common in late game)
Without clamp	Preserves information (high late-game scores), smoother gradient	Values unbounded, less interpretable

For your case (earned scores):

    Early game: earned ~0–50 → (0+14)/(150) to (50+14)/(150) = [0.09, 0.43] ✓ stays in [0,1]
    Mid game: earned ~50–100 → [0.43, 0.76] ✓ stays in [0,1]
    Late game: earned ~100–200 → [0.76, 1.43] ✗ exceeds 1.0

My recommendation:

Don't clamp. Let late-game high scores be 1.2 or 1.3.

Why:

    Late-game scores carry signal (high score in round 5 matters differently than round 1)
    Network can learn to interpret 1.2 vs 1.0 just fine
    Preserves information
    Simpler code (no min(x, 1.0))

encoding[idx + 3] = (game.players[1].earned + 14) / 150.0
encoding[idx + 4] = 0.0

No clamping needed. Network handles it fine.

Still thinking about this and learning how the net works. When the net is combining features together does it also add a constant to each?
3:56 PM
Claude responded: Excellent question.

Excellent question. Yes, the network adds constants (biases) at each layer.
python

# Typical neural network layer:
z = W @ x + b    # W = weights, x = inputs, b = bias (constant)
output = activation(z)

# Example with 2 inputs:
z = w1*input1 + w2*input2 + b
output = ReLU(z)

So for your encoding:
python

# Layer 1 (first hidden layer):
z = w_col*column_ratio + w_row*row_ratio + w_myscore*my_score + ... + b_1

# The bias b_1 is added to everything
# It's learned during backprop, just like weights

Why this matters for your encoding:

If you have all features in [0, 1] range, the bias learns an offset.

Example:

If all features are 0.5 (neutral state):
z = w1*0.5 + w2*0.5 + ... + b
The bias can represent "this baseline state is good or bad"

With your earned score formula:

earned = -14 → (earned + 14) / 100 = 0.0
earned = 0 → (earned + 14) / 100 = 0.14

At earned = -14 (worst case), the feature is exactly 0.0
The network's bias in layer 1 handles "what does 0.0 in all positions mean"

This actually makes your +14 shift even smarter:

    It maps the worst case (earned -14) to 0.0
    The network's bias learns "zero input values = bad baseline"
    This is cleaner than the bias having to learn "interpret negative values as bad"

Does this change anything?

No, your approach is still correct. The bias handles the constant offset at each layer. Your normalization just sets up clean 0.0 anchors for worst-case values.

Does this help clarify how the network will use your encoded values?

The Real Question

"If the net is going to learn to add 14 anyway..."

You're right that the network could learn this, but:

    It costs learning capacity - the network spends representational power learning an offset
    Less stable early in training - large ranges [0, 2.14] vs [0, 1] make gradient flow messier
    We're throwing away symmetry - penalty differential is naturally symmetric around 0, why encode it as [0, 2.14]?

My Recommendation: Use Score Differentials

For penalty and earned, use differentials:
python

# ========== Penalty Differential (1 value) ==========
my_penalty = game.players[0].penalty
opp_penalty = game.players[1].penalty
penalty_diff = my_penalty - opp_penalty  # Range [-28, +14]

# Normalize to [0, 1] with 0.5 as neutral
encoding[idx] = 0.5 + (penalty_diff / 56.0) * 0.5  # Rough scaling
# When my_penalty = -14, opp = 0: diff = -14, value = 0.5 - 0.125 = 0.375
# When my_penalty = 0, opp = -14: diff = +14, value = 0.5 + 0.125 = 0.625

# ========== Earned Differential (1 value) ==========
earned_diff = my_earned - opp_earned  # Range roughly [-200, +200]
encoding[idx] = 0.5 + (earned_diff / 400.0)  # Normalize to [0, 1] with headroom

Why this is better:

    Keeps range [0, 1] (cleaner for network)
    0.5 = neutral (symmetric), not arbitrary
    Uses differential directly (what the network cares about)
    Saves encoding space (2 values instead of 4)

Next question is more on pattern line filling. 
Say I have 4 tiles in a line 5 pattern that will complete a column. That is entered as a feature 0.8 but it is worth nothing until it crosses the threshold to 1.0. How does the net learn that 0.99 is nothing but 1.0 is something
4:06 PM
Claude responded: Excellent question.

Excellent question. This is a real issue—you're identifying a discontinuity.

Pattern line fill = 4/5 = 0.8
→ encoding: 0.8
→ points earned: 0 (nothing happens)

Pattern line fill = 5/5 = 1.0
→ encoding: 1.0
→ points earned: placement_points + adjacencies (real value!)

The net sees a continuous feature [0, 1], but the actual value is discontinuous.

Can the network learn this?

Yes, but with caveats:
python

# The network will see patterns like:
[...fill=0.9, ...] → game outcome mediocre
[...fill=1.0, ...] → game outcome much better

After millions of examples, backprop learns:
"when fill approaches 1.0, outcomes jump"

It learns a sharp non-linearity through its hidden layers

But this is inefficient learning because:

    The net wastes representational power learning this threshold
    Early in training, the gradient from 0.99 → 1.0 is noisy
    The relationship isn't linear—it's a step function

Better Approach: Encode the Threshold

Instead of just fill ratio, encode both:

    Fill ratio (0.0–1.0)
    Is it complete? (0.0 or 1.0 binary flag)

python

# Current (just ratio):
encoding[idx] = tiles_on_line / capacity  # 0.8 for 4/5

# Better (ratio + threshold):
encoding[idx] = tiles_on_line / capacity          # 0.8 for 4/5
encoding[idx + 1] = 1.0 if tiles_on_line == capacity else 0.0  # 0.0 for 4/5, 1.0 for 5/5

Now the network sees:

[fill=0.4, complete=0.0] → no points
[fill=0.8, complete=0.0] → no points (but closer!)
[fill=1.0, complete=1.0] → points! (the flag makes it clear)

The binary flag directly encodes the discontinuity.

for row in range(5):
    is_complete = 1.0 if len(self.pattern_lines[row]) == (row + 1) else 0.0
    features.append(is_complete)  # 5 completion flags

    encoding[idx] = 1.0, encoding[idx+5] = 1.0 → "5 tiles on line 5, complete!"

The binary completion flag helps the network immediately recognize the threshold, while the fill ratio still carries useful information about "how close are we?"

96–100:   Earned scores (2) + padding (3)
101–105:  Floor penalties (2) + padding (3)
106–110:  Bonus points (2) + padding (3)

Total: 111 values

All features:

    Raw, no post-processing (negatives allowed)
    Divisor of 100 for interpretability
    Earned and penalties can go negative
    Network learns to adjust as needed

Claude responded: Good question.

Good question. Let me walk through what happens after the encoding is passed to the network.
python

# You give the net: encoding [111 values]
# Range: mostly [0, 1], some negative (penalties, earned), some > 1 (late-game scores)

encoding = np.array([0.45, 0.82, -0.05, 0.12, ..., 0.98])  # shape: (111,)

# Network's first layer (input → hidden):
hidden_1 = ReLU(W_1 @ encoding + b_1)
# W_1 shape: (hidden_dim, 111)  e.g., (256, 111)
# encoding shape: (111,)
# Result: (256,) values, passed through ReLU

# Second layer (hidden → hidden):
hidden_2 = ReLU(W_2 @ hidden_1 + b_2)
# W_2 shape: (hidden_dim, 256)
# Result: (256,) values

# ... more hidden layers ...

# Output layer (hidden → output):
output = W_out @ hidden_last + b_out
# Output could be:
# - Single value: Q-value estimate (value head)
# - Multiple values: policy logits, value estimates, etc.

What the network actually does:

    Learns weights and biases via backprop that turn your 111 input values into useful intermediate representations
    Each layer performs: weighted sum + bias + activation
    Output depends on your task:
        For a value net: single scalar output (estimate of position value)
        For policy: 180 outputs (one per legal move)
        For both: multiple heads

The Key Question You're Asking

"What does the net do with [0, 1] range inputs?"

Answer: It doesn't care about the absolute range.
python

# These two encodings are effectively the same to the network:
encoding_A = [0.5, 0.3, 0.8, ...]
encoding_B = [50.0, 30.0, 80.0, ...]

# Because the first layer learns weights that scale and shift:
z = W @ encoding_A + b
vs
z = (W/100) @ encoding_B + b

# Same information, just different weight magnitudes

The network will:

    Learn weights of appropriate magnitude for [0, 1] inputs
    Use biases to shift values around
    Use ReLU to create non-linearities
    Learn how features interact (e.g., "high my_score + high opp_score = neutral, high my_score + low opp_score = winning")

Why [0, 1] Range Still Matters

Even though the network can learn any scale, [0, 1] is still better because:

    Gradient flow: Features in similar scales get similar gradient magnitudes. If one feature is [0, 100] and another is [0, 1], gradients can be imbalanced.
    Weight initialization: Standard initialization (Xavier, He) assumes inputs are roughly [0, 1]. Out-of-range values can cause dead ReLU neurons early in training.
    Training stability: Smaller input ranges = smaller weight updates needed = more stable training.

python

# If input is 50.0, first layer gradient might be huge:
loss = f(W @ input + b)
d_loss/d_W = input * d_loss/d_b  # input=50 makes this 50x larger

# If input is 0.5, gradient is smaller and more stable:
d_loss/d_W = input * d_loss/d_b  # input=0.5 makes this manageable

So For Your Encoding

You're mostly [0, 1] with some negatives (penalties) and some > 1 (late-game scores).

This is fine. The network will:

    Learn weights appropriate for this range
    Handle the few negatives naturally (negative input × negative weight = positive)
    Handle > 1 values fine (larger inputs just need larger weights)

But ideally, you'd keep everything tighter [0, 1] or at least [-1, 1] to avoid the gradient scaling issues.
Does This Answer Your Question?

Or are you asking something more specific about how the network uses the features to make decisions?

Claude responded: Azul AlphaZero → Value Net Learning — Project Plan (Updated)
Azul AlphaZero → Value Net Learning — Project Plan (Updated)

    Status: Phase redesign complete (pivot from AlphaZero to supervised value learning) Last updated: 2026-05-09 Goal: Build a superhuman Azul bot via learned value function + AlphaBeta search

Vision Shift

Original goal: AlphaZero-style self-play training → strong bot

Reality: Neural policy head stagnated (gen 1–2). MCTS distributions near-random. Training signal corrupted.

New approach: Abandon policy learning. Use supervised value learning instead.

    AlphaBeta search provides ground-truth position evaluation
    Train neural value net to match AB evaluations
    Integrate learned value into AB search
    Iterate: each generation's net produces better supervision for the next

Expected outcome: 90%+ win rate vs AB hard. Superhuman Azul play.
Architecture: AlphaBeta + Learned Value

Classical AlphaBeta search with two evaluation options:

Hand-Coded Eval (baseline):
  value = player.earned_differential (current score advantage)
  
Learned Value (gen-0+):
  encoding = encode_game(position)
  value = value_net(encoding)  [trained on AB supervision]
  
Integration:
  AlphaBetaWithLearnedValue(depth=3, threshold=8, net_path="gen_0000.pt")
    ├─ Searches to depth 3 or until round boundary
    ├─ Uses net prediction at leaf nodes (instead of hand-coded eval)
    └─ Returns best move

Why this works:

    AlphaBeta is fast, deterministic, correct
    Hand-coded eval has constant signal but limited strategic understanding
    Neural value net learns to refine evaluation (cleanness, column strategy, end-game dynamics)
    Generational improvement: gen-1 net sees better AB evaluations, produces better supervision for gen-2

Normalization Principles:

    All divisors use 100 for human interpretability (0.66 = 66 points)
    Scores, earned, bonuses: raw division by 100 (can exceed 1.0 in late game)
    Penalties: raw division by 100 (range [-0.14, 0.0], negative values allowed)
    Ratios: division by their max (0.0–1.0 range)
    No post-processing, no shifts, no inversions
    Network learns to adjust negative values as needed

Data Generation Strategy
Phase 0: Supervision Data (Days 1–2)

Algorithm: Game playthrough with exhaustive search at round boundaries
python

def generate_supervision_data(supervisor_agent, num_examples, output_file=None, threshold=5):
    """
    Generate supervision examples from random games.
    
    For smoke test (gen-0): store in RAM (< 10k examples)
    For scaling (gen-1+): stream to disk (Option 3 hybrid approach)
    
    Args:
        supervisor_agent: AlphaBetaAgent or AlphaBetaWithLearnedValue
        num_examples: target number of training examples
        output_file: if provided, stream to disk; if None, keep in RAM
        threshold: exhaustive search when <= this many moves remain in round
    
    Returns:
        training_data: list of (encoding, value) tuples (if RAM mode)
        OR output_file path (if disk mode)
    """
    training_data = []  # RAM mode (gen-0 smoke test)
    examples_collected = 0
    
    for game_idx in range(num_games_estimate):
        game = Game(seed=random())
        
        while not game.is_game_over():
            # Play randomly until near round boundary
            while not game.is_round_over():
                moves = game.legal_moves()
                game.make_move(random.choice(moves))
                game.advance(skip_setup=True)
                
                # Check: within threshold of round end?
                remaining_moves = estimate_remaining_moves(game)
                if remaining_moves <= threshold:  # threshold=5 or 6
                    # Run exhaustive AB search to round boundary
                    ab = AlphaBetaAgent(depth=999, threshold=0)  # Force full search
                    ab_tree = ab.build_complete_tree(game)
                    
                    # Extract all visited nodes
                    for node in ab_tree.all_nodes():
                        if node.was_visited:  # Skip pruned branches
                            encoding = encode_game(node.game_state)
                            value = node.value
                            
                            training_data.append((encoding, value))
                            examples_collected += 1
                            
                            # For disk streaming (gen-1+):
                            # if examples_collected % 1000 == 0:
                            #     _flush_to_disk(training_data, output_file, append=True)
                            #     training_data = []
            
            # Round boundary reached; continue to next round
            game.advance()
        
        # Game over
        if examples_collected >= num_examples:
            break
    
    # Final flush for disk mode
    if output_file and training_data:
        _flush_to_disk(training_data, output_file, append=True)
    
    return training_data if not output_file else output_file

Key points:

    Play full random games to natural termination
    When ≤ 5 moves remain in a round: exhaustive AB search (depth=999, threshold=0)
    AB's pruning is trusted: if a branch is pruned, assume generalization will also skip it
    Collect all visited nodes from the AB tree
    Transitive benefit: depth-4 search implicitly gives us depth-3, depth-2, depth-1 data

Storage modes:

    Gen-0 (smoke test): RAM-only, ~7 MB for 10k examples
    Gen-1+ (scaling): Stream to disk every 1000 examples, load via PyTorch DataLoader

Wall time estimate:

    5k examples: 1–2 hours
    10k examples: 4–6 hours (overnight acceptable)
    20k examples: 12+ hours (full day)

Smoke test approach: Start with 5k–10k, evaluate win rate. Scale up for later generations if needed.
Training Pipeline: Value Net
Phase 1: Supervised Value Learning (Days 2–4)

Architecture:
python

class AzulValueNet(nn.Module):
    """
    Neural value network for Azul position evaluation.
    
    Architecture:
      input (111) → Linear (64) → ResBlock (64) → Dropout (0.1)
                 → value_head (64 → 32 → 1)
    
    Output: Single scalar value per position
      - Represents: (my_earned - opponent_earned) / 100.0
      - Range: approximately [-0.14, 3.0+]
    """
    
    def __init__(self, input_size=111, hidden_dim=64, dropout=0.1):
        super().__init__()
        self.trunk = nn.Sequential(
            nn.Linear(input_size, hidden_dim),
            ResBlock(hidden_dim),
            nn.Dropout(dropout),
        )
        self.value_head = nn.Sequential(
            nn.Linear(hidden_dim, 32),
            nn.ReLU(),
            nn.Linear(32, 1),
        )
    
    def forward(self, encoding):
        """
        Args:
            encoding: (batch_size, 111) tensor
        
        Returns:
            value: (batch_size,) tensor of scalar values
        """
        trunk_out = self.trunk(encoding)
        value = self.value_head(trunk_out).squeeze(-1)
        return value

Training Loop:
python

def train_value_net(train_loader, val_loader, max_epochs=200, 
                    early_stopping_patience=20, lr=1e-3, 
                    save_dir="checkpoints/gen_0000/", device='cuda'):
    """
    Supervised training: minimize MSE between net predictions and AB labels.
    
    Args:
        train_loader: DataLoader with (encoding, ab_value) pairs
        val_loader: DataLoader for validation
        max_epochs: maximum epochs (early stopping usually stops earlier)
        early_stopping_patience: stop if val_loss doesn't improve for N epochs
        lr: learning rate
        save_dir: where to save checkpoint and logs
        device: 'cuda' or 'cpu'
    
    Returns:
        best_checkpoint_path: path to model with best val_loss
    """
    net = AzulValueNet(input_size=111).to(device)
    optimizer = torch.optim.Adam(net.parameters(), lr=lr)
    loss_fn = nn.MSELoss()
    
    best_val_loss = float('inf')
    patience_counter = 0
    training_log = []
    
    for epoch in range(max_epochs):
        # ========== Training Phase ==========
        net.train()
        train_loss = 0.0
        
        for batch_encoding, batch_values in train_loader:
            batch_encoding = batch_encoding.to(device)
            batch_values = batch_values.to(device)
            
            optimizer.zero_grad()
            pred = net(batch_encoding)
            loss = loss_fn(pred, batch_values)
            loss.backward()
            optimizer.step()
            train_loss += loss.item()
        
        train_loss /= len(train_loader)
        
        # ========== Validation Phase ==========
        net.eval()
        val_loss = 0.0
        val_mae = 0.0
        val_preds = []
        val_targets = []
        
        with torch.no_grad():
            for batch_encoding, batch_values in val_loader:
                batch_encoding = batch_encoding.to(device)
                batch_values = batch_values.to(device)
                
                pred = net(batch_encoding)
                loss = loss_fn(pred, batch_values)
                val_loss += loss.item()
                val_mae += torch.abs(pred - batch_values).mean().item()
                
                val_preds.extend(pred.cpu().numpy())
                val_targets.extend(batch_values.cpu().numpy())
        
        val_loss /= len(val_loader)
        val_mae /= len(val_loader)
        
        # Compute Pearson correlation
        val_preds = np.array(val_preds)
        val_targets = np.array(val_targets)
        val_correlation = np.corrcoef(val_preds, val_targets)[0, 1]
        if np.isnan(val_correlation):
            val_correlation = 0.0
        
        # ========== Logging ==========
        log_entry = {
            'epoch': epoch,
            'train_loss': train_loss,
            'val_loss': val_loss,
            'val_mae': val_mae,
            'val_correlation': val_correlation,
            'learning_rate': lr,
        }
        training_log.append(log_entry)
        
        print(f"Epoch {epoch:3d} | train_loss={train_loss:.4f} | val_loss={val_loss:.4f} | "
              f"mae={val_mae:.4f} | corr={val_correlation:.3f}")
        
        # ========== Early Stopping ==========
        if val_loss < best_val_loss:
            best_val_loss = val_loss
            patience_counter = 0
            best_checkpoint = f"{save_dir}/best_checkpoint.pt"
            torch.save(net.state_dict(), best_checkpoint)
            print(f"  → New best val_loss={val_loss:.4f}, saved checkpoint")
        else:
            patience_counter += 1
            if patience_counter >= early_stopping_patience:
                print(f"Early stopping at epoch {epoch} (patience exhausted)")
                break
    
    # ========== Save Summary ==========
    os.makedirs(save_dir, exist_ok=True)
    
    # Save training log
    import csv
    with open(f"{save_dir}/training_log.csv", 'w', newline='') as f:
        writer = csv.DictWriter(f, fieldnames=training_log[0].keys())
        writer.writeheader()
        writer.writerows(training_log)
    
    # Save summary
    with open(f"{save_dir}/summary.txt", 'w') as f:
        f.write(f"Generation Training Summary\n")
        f.write(f"Best epoch: {training_log.index(min(training_log, key=lambda x: x['val_loss']))}\n")
        f.write(f"Best val_loss: {best_val_loss:.6f}\n")
        f.write(f"Final train_loss: {training_log[-1]['train_loss']:.6f}\n")
        f.write(f"Final val_correlation: {training_log[-1]['val_correlation']:.4f}\n")
        f.write(f"Total epochs: {len(training_log)}\n")
    
    # Generate plots
    plot_training_curves(training_log, save_dir)
    
    return best_checkpoint

Hyperparameters:

    Input size: 111 (encoder size, locked)
    Hidden dim: 64 (small, effective)
    Dropout: 0.1 (mild regularization)
    Learning rate: 1e-3 (standard for supervised regression)
    Batch size: 128
    Train/val split: 80/20
    Max epochs: 200
    Early stopping patience: 20 epochs

Logging (per epoch):

    train_loss, val_loss, val_mae, val_correlation, learning_rate
    Save to CSV: checkpoints/gen_NNNN/training_log.csv

Checkpointing:

    Save best checkpoint at lowest val_loss
    Save final summary: summary.txt (best_epoch, best_val_loss, final_metrics)

Visualization (post-training):

    loss_curves.png — train vs val loss over epochs
    metrics_curves.png — val MAE + val correlation
    All generated by matplotlib, saved to checkpoints/gen_NNNN/

Evaluation & Generational Loop
Phase 2: Evaluation (Every generation)

Test 1: vs Hand-Coded AlphaBeta
python

def evaluate_agents(agent_a, agent_b, num_mirror_pairs=100, verbose=True):
    """
    Run mirrored pair evaluation.
    
    For each pair:
      Game 1: agent_a (player 0) vs agent_b (player 1), bag_seed S
      Game 2: agent_b (player 0) vs agent_a (player 1), bag_seed S (sides swapped)
    
    This controls for first-player advantage and randomness.
    
    Args:
        agent_a: first agent
        agent_b: second agent
        num_mirror_pairs: number of mirrored pairs to play (2 games per pair)
        verbose: print progress
    
    Returns:
        win_rate: fraction of games won by agent_a (0.0 to 1.0)
    """
    wins = 0
    total_games = 0
    
    for pair_idx in range(num_mirror_pairs):
        bag_seed = random()
        
        # Game 1: A vs B (A is player 0)
        game1 = Game(seed=bag_seed)
        winner1_idx = play_game(game1, [agent_a, agent_b])
        if winner1_idx == 0:
            wins += 1
        total_games += 1
        
        # Game 2: B vs A (A is player 1, sides swapped)
        game2 = Game(seed=bag_seed)
        winner2_idx = play_game(game2, [agent_b, agent_a])
        if winner2_idx == 1:  # A won (player 1 in this game)
            wins += 1
        total_games += 1
        
        if verbose and (pair_idx + 1) % 10 == 0:
            print(f"Completed {pair_idx + 1}/{num_mirror_pairs} pairs "
                  f"({total_games} games, {wins}/{total_games} wins for A)")
    
    win_rate = wins / total_games
    return win_rate

Test 2: vs AlphaBeta Hard (Progression Tracking)
python

# Every generation, also test against AB hard
win_rate_vs_hard = evaluate_agents(
    AlphaBetaWithLearnedValue(depth=3, net_path=f"gen_{generation:04d}.pt"),
    AlphaBetaAgent(depth=3, threshold=8),  # AB hard
    num_mirror_pairs=100,
    verbose=True
)

Parameters:

    Format: 100 mirrored pairs (200 games, controls for luck)
    Depth: configurable, defaults to same as supervision depth (3)
    Win rate threshold: 55% to declare "success"

Phase 3: Generational Loop (Days 5–14)
python

def generational_loop(max_generations=10):
    """
    Automated generational loop: supervise → train → eval → decide.
    
    Each generation:
      1. Generate supervision data using current best net (or hand-coded for gen-0)
      2. Train new net on supervision
      3. Evaluate new net vs baseline and vs AB hard
      4. Log results, decide if improvement warrants next generation
    
    Stopping criteria:
      - max_generations reached
      - < 2% improvement for 3 consecutive generations (plateau)
      - User keyboard interrupt (graceful stop)
    """
    win_rates = {}
    plateau_counter = 0
    
    for generation in range(max_generations):
        print(f"\n{'='*60}")
        print(f"Generation {generation}")
        print(f"{'='*60}")
        
        # ========== Step 1: Generate Supervision ==========
        if generation == 0:
            supervisor = AlphaBetaAgent(depth=3, threshold=0)
            print(f"Supervisor: Hand-coded AlphaBeta (depth=3, full search to round end)")
        else:
            supervisor = AlphaBetaWithLearnedValue(
                depth=3,
                threshold=0,
                net_path=f"checkpoints/gen_{generation-1:04d}/best_checkpoint.pt"
            )
            print(f"Supervisor: gen_{generation-1:04d} value net (depth=3)")
        
        target_examples = 10000 if generation < 3 else 15000  # Scale up after smoke test
        print(f"Generating {target_examples} supervision examples...")
        
        try:
            train_data, val_data = generate_supervision_data(
                supervisor,
                num_examples=target_examples,
                output_file=None if generation == 0 else f"checkpoints/gen_{generation:04d}/train_data.pt",
                threshold=5,
            )
            print(f"Generated {len(train_data)} training examples")
        except KeyboardInterrupt:
            print(f"Interrupted during data generation. Stopping.")
            break
        
        # ========== Step 2: Train Value Net ==========
        print(f"Training gen_{generation:04d} value net...")
        
        try:
            best_checkpoint = train_value_net(
                train_data,
                val_data,
                max_epochs=200,
                early_stopping_patience=20,
                lr=1e-3,
                save_dir=f"checkpoints/gen_{generation:04d}/",
            )
            print(f"Training complete. Best checkpoint: {best_checkpoint}")
        except KeyboardInterrupt:
            print(f"Interrupted during training. Stopping.")
            break
        
        # ========== Step 3: Evaluate vs Baseline ==========
        print(f"Evaluating gen_{generation:04d} vs hand-coded AB...")
        
        try:
            win_rate_baseline = evaluate_agents(
                AlphaBetaWithLearnedValue(depth=3, net_path=best_checkpoint),
                AlphaBetaAgent(depth=3, threshold=8),
                num_mirror_pairs=100,
                verbose=True,
            )
            print(f"Gen {generation} vs hand-coded: {win_rate_baseline*100:.1f}%")
        except KeyboardInterrupt:
            print(f"Interrupted during evaluation. Stopping.")
            break
        
        # ========== Step 4: Evaluate vs AB Hard ==========
        print(f"Evaluating gen_{generation:04d} vs AB hard...")
        
        try:
            win_rate_hard = evaluate_agents(
                AlphaBetaWithLearnedValue(depth=3, net_path=best_checkpoint),
                AlphaBetaAgent(depth=3, threshold=8),
                num_mirror_pairs=100,
                verbose=True,
            )
            print(f"Gen {generation} vs AB hard: {win_rate_hard*100:.1f}%")
        except KeyboardInterrupt:
            print(f"Interrupted during evaluation. Stopping.")
            break
        
        win_rates[generation] = {
            'vs_baseline': win_rate_baseline,
            'vs_hard': win_rate_hard,
        }
        
        # ========== Step 5: Analyze Progress ==========
        if generation > 0:
            prev_rate = win_rates[generation-1]['vs_baseline']
            improvement = win_rate_baseline - prev_rate
            print(f"\nImprovement over gen {generation-1}: {improvement*100:+.1f}%")
            
            if improvement < 0.02:  # < 2% improvement
                plateau_counter += 1
                print(f"Plateau warning ({plateau_counter}/3)")
                if plateau_counter >= 3:
                    print(f"Plateau detected for 3 consecutive generations. Stopping.")
                    break
            else:
                plateau_counter = 0  # Reset if we see improvement
        
        # ========== Step 6: User Confirmation ==========
        print(f"\n{'='*60}")
        if generation < max_generations - 1:
            try:
                prompt = f"Proceed to gen {generation+1}? (y/n/skip): "
                response = input(prompt).lower().strip()
                if response == 'n':
                    print(f"Stopping at gen {generation}.")
                    break
                elif response == 'skip':
                    print(f"Skipping eval games for gen {generation+1}.")
                    # (could implement skipping here)
            except KeyboardInterrupt:
                print(f"\nInterrupted. Saving results up to gen {generation}...")
                break
    
    # ========== Summary ==========
    print(f"\n{'='*60}")
    print(f"Training Complete")
    print(f"{'='*60}")
    for gen, rates in sorted(win_rates.items()):
        print(f"Gen {gen:2d}: baseline={rates['vs_baseline']*100:5.1f}%, "
              f"hard={rates['vs_hard']*100:5.1f}%")
    
    # Find best generation
    best_gen = max(win_rates.keys(), key=lambda g: win_rates[g]['vs_hard'])
    print(f"\nBest generation: Gen {best_gen} (vs AB hard: {win_rates[best_gen]['vs_hard']*100:.1f}%)")
    print(f"Checkpoint: checkpoints/gen_{best_gen:04d}/best_checkpoint.pt")

Key decisions:

    Max generations: 10 (hard cap)
    Data per generation:
        Gen 0–2: 10k examples (speed, smoke test)
        Gen 3+: 15k–20k examples (refinement)
    Plateau detection: < 2% improvement vs previous gen, 3 times in a row
    Keyboard interrupt: Graceful stop with Ctrl+C, saves results
    Evaluation: Every generation, 100 mirror pairs vs baseline + vs hard

Web Dashboard: Training Orchestration
Phase 5b: Training Dashboard (Days 6–8, parallel to Phase 5)

Architecture:

Frontend (Vanilla JS):
  ├─ Single-page dashboard (training_dashboard.html)
  ├─ Live polling of /training/status (every 2 seconds)
  ├─ Real-time metric updates, plots, controls
  └─ Manual start/stop/pause for each phase

Backend (FastAPI):
  ├─ Extended api/main.py with /training/* endpoints
  ├─ File-based state persistence (pipeline_state.json)
  ├─ Serves plots and generation history
  └─ Handles start/stop/pause signals

Orchestrator (Background Worker):
  ├─ Watches pipeline state file
  ├─ Runs generation → training → eval sequence
  ├─ Updates state in real-time
  └─ Graceful signal handling

Storage: File-based (per generation)

checkpoints/

├─ training_settings.json        ← User settings
└─ gen_NNNN/
   ├─ status.json               ← Phase, progress, metrics

   ├─ training_log.csv
   ├─ loss_curves.png
   ├─ metrics_curves.png
   ├─ summary.txt
   └─ live_metrics.json         ← Rolling window

Frontend: frontend/training_dashboard.html

Single-page HTML5 dashboard with:

    Status card: Current phase (idle/generating/training/evaluating), progress bar
    Live metrics: Games collected, epoch, train loss, val loss, correlation
    Control panel: Start/stop buttons for each phase (enabled based on phase)
    Settings form: Target examples, depth, LR, batch size (editable, saved to backend)
    Generation table: History of all generations with stats
    Plots: Loss curves and metrics curves (auto-updated)

Key features:

    Vanilla JS (no build tools needed)
    Live polling of /training/status endpoint
    Graceful error handling
    Responsive layout

Feature Extraction Architecture
Player Features

Player owns and maintains all player-specific features:
python

# engine/player.py

class Player:
    def __init__(self, player_idx: int):
        # Board state
        self.wall: List[List[int]] = [[0] * 5 for _ in range(5)]
        self.pattern_lines: List[List[Tile]] = [[] for _ in range(5)]
        self.floor: List[Tile] = []
        
        # Scoring caches
        self.score = 0
        self.pending = 0
        self.penalty = 0
        self.bonus = 0
        
        # Feature caches (updated on state change)
        self._column_completion_ratios = np.array([0.0] * 5, dtype=np.float32)
        self._row_completion_ratios = np.array([0.0] * 5, dtype=np.float32)
        self._pattern_line_fill_ratios = np.array([0.0] * 5, dtype=np.float32)
        self._pattern_line_cleanness = np.array([0.0] * 5, dtype=np.float32)
        self._pattern_lines_will_be_empty = np.array([0.0] * 5, dtype=np.float32)
        self._incomplete_lines_per_color = np.array([0.0] * 5, dtype=np.float32)
        self._max_column_completion = 0.0
        self._second_max_column_completion = 0.0
        self._top_row = 0.0
        self._second_top_row = 0.0
        self._third_top_row = 0.0
        self._total_pattern_tiles = 0.0
    
    # Property access (read-only)
    @property
    def column_completion_ratios(self) -> np.ndarray:
        return self._column_completion_ratios.copy()
    
    # ... more properties ...
    
    # Feature updates (called when state changes)
    def _update_column_features(self):
        """Recalculate column-based features."""
        for col in range(5):
            filled = sum(self.wall[row][col] for row in range(5))
            self._column_completion_ratios[col] = filled / 5.0
        
        self._max_column_completion = float(np.max(self._column_completion_ratios))
        self._second_max_column_completion = float(
            np.partition(self._column_completion_ratios, -2)[-2]
        )
    
    # ... more update methods ...
    
    # Feature extraction
    def extract_features(self) -> np.ndarray:
        """
        Extract all player-specific features as a 35-value vector.
        
        Returns: 35-value array
          - Columns: 7 values
          - Rows: 8 values
          - Pattern lines: 15 values
          - Line flexibility: 5 values
        """
        features = []
        
        # Columns (7)
        features.extend(self._column_completion_ratios)  # 5
        features.append(self._max_column_completion)  # 1
        features.append(self._second_max_column_completion)  # 1
        
        # Rows (8)
        features.extend(self._row_completion_ratios)  # 5
        features.append(self._top_row)  # 1
        features.append(self._second_top_row)  # 1
        features.append(self._third_top_row)  # 1
        
        # Pattern lines (15)
        features.extend(self._pattern_line_fill_ratios)  # 5
        features.append(self._total_pattern_tiles)  # 1
        features.extend(self._pattern_line_cleanness)  # 5
        features.extend(self._pattern_lines_will_be_empty)  # 5
        
        # Line flexibility (5)
        features.extend(self._incomplete_lines_per_color)  # 5
        
        return np.array(features, dtype=np.float32)

Game-Level Features

Game assembles both players' features and game-level features:
python

# engine/game.py

class Game:
    def extract_features(self) -> np.ndarray:
        """
        Extract all game features as a 111-value vector.
        
        Returns: 111-value array
          - Player 0 features: 35
          - Player 1 features: 35
          - Game-level features: 6
          - Tile availability: 5
          - Source counts: 5
          - Bag contents: 5
          - Official scores: 5
          - Earned scores: 5
          - Floor penalties: 5
          - Bonus points: 5
        """
        features = []
        idx = 0
        
        # Player features (70)
        features.extend(self.players[0].extract_features())  # 35
        features.extend(self.players[1].extract_features())  # 35
        
        # Game-level features (6)
        features.append(1.0 if self.first_player_idx == 0 else 0.0)
        features.append(1.0 if self.first_player_idx == 1 else 0.0)
        features.append(self._can_player_end_game(0))
        features.append(self._can_player_end_game(1))
        features.append((6 - self.round_num) / 6.0)
        features.append(0.0)  # Padding
        
        # Tile availability (5)
        availability = self.tile_availability()
        for color_idx in range(5):
            features.append(availability[color_idx][0] / 20.0)
        
        # Source counts (5)
        for color_idx in range(5):
            features.append(availability[color_idx][1] / 5.0)
        
        # Bag contents (5)
        bag_contents = [0] * 5
        for tile in self.bag:
            bag_contents[tile.tile] += 1
        for color_idx in range(5):
            features.append(bag_contents[color_idx] / 20.0)
        
        # Official scores (5)
        features.append(self.players[0].score / 100.0)
        features.append(self.players[1].score / 100.0)
        features.append(0.0)
        features.append(0.0)
        features.append(0.0)
        
        # Earned scores (5)
        features.append(self.players[0].earned / 100.0)
        features.append(self.players[1].earned / 100.0)
        features.append(0.0)
        features.append(0.0)
        features.append(0.0)
        
        # Floor penalties (5)
        features.append(self.players[0].penalty / 100.0)
        features.append(self.players[1].penalty / 100.0)
        features.append(0.0)
        features.append(0.0)
        features.append(0.0)
        
        # Bonus points (5)
        features.append(self.players[0].bonus / 100.0)
        features.append(self.players[1].bonus / 100.0)
        features.append(0.0)
        features.append(0.0)
        features.append(0.0)
        
        return np.array(features, dtype=np.float32)

Encoder

Encoder simply calls game.extract_features():
python

# neural/encoder.py

def encode_game(game: Game) -> np.ndarray:
    """
    Encode game state as 111-value flat vector.
    
    All values raw (no post-processing, negatives allowed):
    - Ratios: [0, 1]
    - Scores: [0, 2+] (late-game values can exceed 1.0)
    - Penalties: [-0.14, 0.0]
    - Earned: [-0.14, 2+] (can be negative)
    
    Network learns to interpret all ranges appropriately.
    """
    return game.extract_features()

Hard-Won Lessons (Updated)

Building on experience from AlphaZero phase:

    Supervised learning is simpler than RL. Ground-truth labels (AB evaluations) are reliable. No credit assignment horror.
    Value learning >> policy learning for Azul. Move selection (policy) is already solved by AlphaBeta. What's missing is position evaluation (value).
    Mirrored pairs eliminate luck. Azul has high variance. Always use mirrored pairs for evaluation.
    Early stopping is your friend. Don't manually set epochs. Let validation loss guide training.
    Generational improvement is compounding but plateaus fast. Expect 5–10% improvement per gen early, then 1–2% later. Plateau is normal.
    Exhaustive search at round boundaries is the right data. When factories empty and pattern lines are committed, the value
